The Two Modified Propositions and the Question
Sanusi explains the correct and false interpretations of negation placement and the non-obligatory answer to a non-exhaustive question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct interpretation of the two modified propositions and why answering a non-exhaustive disjunction is not obligatory.

Core Idea
Not every disjunctive question demands an answer; only exhaustive ones do.

Sanusi discusses a subtle logical point: when the 'insofar as' is placed before negation, it yields a privative affirmative which is false. He then considers the case of being asked about the 'two modified propositions' – affirmative propositions with negative predicates. The question 'Is it? or is it not?' is not exhaustive because the essence may imply neither, so we are not obliged to answer. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once silently, then read the literal translation. Notice the terms: rabṭa, salb, ījāb ʿudūlī, maʿdūlatayn.
The passage is dense; focus on the flow of argument.
2
Identify the two cases of negation placement
The passage contrasts two cases: (1) the copula after negation (correct – negation of copula) and (2) 'insofar as' before negation (false – privative affirmative). Mark these distinctions.
These are foundational for understanding modified propositions.
3
Understand the problem with the question
When asked 'Is it? or is it not?' using the two modified propositions, the disjunction is not exclusive because the essence might not imply either. Thus, no answer is obligatory. Voluntary answer: 'neither this nor that'.
Compare with the two extremes of contradiction (exclusive).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقتضي؛ وذلك لأن الرابطة هاهنا متأخرة عن السلب فالمقصود سلب الربط، وهو حق. ومعنى تقديم الحيثية على حرف السلب أنها إذا أخذت بهذه الحيثية تقتضي لا، وذلك لأن الرابطة في هذه العبارة متقدمة على السلب، فالمتبادر منها الإيجاب العدولي، وهذا باطل. ولو سألنا عن المعدولتين) أراد الموجبتين المعدولة والمحصلة على سبيل التغليب ( فقيل: أهي، أولا لم يلزمنا الجواب عن هذا السؤال؛ لأنه غير حاصر، بخلاف طرفي النقيض؛ إذ لا مخرج عنهما. ( وإن قلنا): أي وإن أجبنا عن هذا السؤال تبرعاً قلنا: لا هذا ولا ذاك) بالمعنى الذي عرفته؛

قوله: (وهو حق لما عرفت من أنها ليست مقتضية لشيء من المتقابلات، وما ذكره صاحب المقاصد من أن الماهية من حيث هي مقتضية للوازمها، فقد عرفت فساده. قوله: (فالمتبادر منها الإيجاب العدولي) أراد بالإيجاب العدولي: الإيجاب الذي يكون السلب جزءاً من المحمول، وتعبير المصنف بلا لإظهار الجزئية، وذلك لأن الجواب قضية سالبة المحمول؛ لما عرفت أن السؤال بطرفي النقيض، فلا يرد أن ليس موضوعة لسلب النسبة، فكيف يكون الإيجاب عدولياً؟ وما قيل من أن الجواب على تقدير التقديم إذا كانت موجبة سالبة المحمول يكون معناه بعينه معنى السالبة البسيطة لما تقرر من أنهما متلازمان، فيكون كلا الجوابين صحيحاً بلا فرق فليس بشيء؛ لأن تلازمهما باعتبار عدم اقتضاء وجود الموضوع لا يقتضي أن لا يكون بينهما فرق بأن يكون معنى إحداهما الاتصاف بالسلب، ومعنى الأخرى سلب الاتصاف.

قوله: (لم يلزمنا الجواب عن هذا السؤال لأن جوابه بالتعيين والتعيين إنما يلزم إذا كان الترديد حاصراً، ولا حصر لجواز أن لا يقتضي شيئاً منهما.

قوله: (لا يقتضي إلخ) ظاهر تفريع قوله: فإذا سئلنا إلخ على ما سبق يقتضي أن يقال: هاهنا معناه أن ليس نفسها ولا داخلا فيها. ويمكن أن يقال مراد المصنف بالاقتضاء الاقتضاء بالعينية أو الجزئية لا مطلقه بقرينة قوله سابقاً لازماً لها ومفارقاً إذ لا يصح نفي مطلق اقتضاء اللواحق اللازمة للماهية ضرورة تحقق اقتضاء الفردية للثلاثة مثلا فحينئذ يتلاءم سابق الكلام ولاحقه، ويندفع ما ذكره في شرح المقاصد من أنه إذا أريد بتقديم الحيثية أن ذلك العارض من مقتضيات الماهية صح في مثل قولنا الأربعة من حيث هي زوج إذ ليست بفرد دون قولنا: الإنسان من حيث هو ضاحك؛ إذ ليس بضاحك، فما ذكر في المواقف من أن تقديم الحيثية على السلب معناه اقتضاء السلب وهو باطل ليس على إطلاقه لا يقال: الاقتضاء بالعينية لا له؛ لأن الاقتضاء نسبة تقتضى المغايرة؛ لأنا نقول: المغايرة الاعتبارية كافية فهي متحققة. قوله: (قلنا لا هذا ولا ذاك فإن قلت: إذا كان معنى هذا الجواب أن الماهية من حيث هي لا هذا ولا ذاك، كان قولاً بأنها تقتضي عدمها لتقدم الحيثية، وقد مر أنه باطل، فإن كان معناه أن الماهية ليست من حيث هي هذا ولا ذاك لم يطابق السؤال، لأن السؤال عن المعدول المرتب.
It implies; and that is because the copula here is after the negation, so the intended is the negation of the copula, and that is correct. And the meaning of placing the 'insofar as' before the negation particle is that when [the essence] is taken with this 'insofar as', it implies 'not', and that is because the copula in this expression is before the negation, so the apparent meaning from it is the privative affirmative (al-ījāb al-ʿudūlī), and that is false. And if we are asked about the two modified propositions (al-maʿdūlatayn) — he meant the two affirmative propositions, the modified and the unmodified, by way of dominant usage — and it is said: 'Is it? or is it not?' we are not obliged to answer this question; because it is not exclusive, unlike the two extremes of contradiction, as there is no escape from them. (And if we say): i.e., if we answer this question voluntarily, we say: 'It is neither this nor that' in the sense you have understood. His statement: (And it is correct, because you know that it does not imply any of the opposites, and what the author of al-Maqāṣid mentioned, that the essence insofar as it is implies its necessary concomitants, you know its falsity.) His statement: (The apparent meaning from it is the privative affirmative) he meant by the privative affirmative: the affirmative in which the negation is part of the predicate, and the author's expression with 'lā' is to show the partitive nature; because the answer is a proposition with a negative predicate, since you know that the question is with the two extremes of contradiction, so it cannot be objected that 'lā' is not designed for negation of the relation, so how can it be a privative affirmative? And what was said that the answer on the basis of placement before, if it is an affirmative with a negative predicate, its meaning is exactly the meaning of the simple negative, since it is established that they are equivalent, so both answers are equally correct, is nothing; because their equivalence with respect to the lack of implication of the existence of the subject does not necessitate that there is no difference between them, such that the meaning of one is being characterized by the negation, and the meaning of the other is the negation of the characterization. His statement: (We are not obliged to answer this question) because answering it requires specification, and specification is only required when the disjunction is exclusive, and there is no exclusivity, because it is possible that [the essence] does not imply either of the two. His statement: ('Does not imply', etc.) The apparent branching of his statement 'So if we are asked, etc.' upon the preceding necessitates that it be said: Here it means that it is not itself nor included in it. And it is possible to say that the author intended by 'implication' implication by way of identity or part, not absolute implication, as indicated by his earlier statement 'a concomitant and a separable', because it is not valid to negate the absolute implication of necessary concomitants from the essence, due to the necessity of the implication of oddness for three, for example. So then the earlier and later speech align, and what was mentioned in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid, that if it is intended by placing the 'insofar as' before that this accident is among the implications of the essence, it would be valid in such as our saying 'Four insofar as it is even is not odd', without our saying 'Man insofar as he is laughing is not laughing', so what was mentioned in al-Mawāqif that placing the 'insofar as' before the negation means implication of the negation, and it is false, is not absolute. It cannot be said: Implication by identity is not valid because implication is a relation that requires otherness; for we say: conceptual otherness is sufficient, and it is realized. His statement: (We say: neither this nor that. If you say: If the meaning of this answer is that the essence insofar as it is is neither this nor that, then it is a statement that it implies its non-existence because of the precedence of the 'insofar as', and it has passed that this is false; then if its meaning is that the essence is not insofar as it is this nor that, it does not match the question, because the question is about the modified related.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a complex refutation and clarification. It begins by explaining the correct interpretation when the negation is placed after the copula: it means negation of the copula, which is correct. Then it contrasts this with placing the 'ḥaythiyya' before the negation, which yields a privative affirmative, which is false. Next, it discusses the case of the 'two modified propositions' (al-maʿdūlatayn) — affirmative propositions with a negative predicate and unmodified affirmative. When asked 'Is it? or is it not?' using these, the answer is not obligatory because the disjunction is not exhaustive; the essence might imply neither. If one voluntarily answers, one says 'neither this nor that' in the sense previously understood. The commentary then criticizes the view of the author of al-Maqāṣid that the essence implies its necessary concomitants, asserting its falsity. It further explains that the 'apparent meaning' of the expression is the privative affirmative, and it defends the author against an objection that the expression 'lā' cannot yield a privative affirmative. It also refutes the claim that the two formulations are equivalent, showing that their equivalence in terms of lack of implication does not erase the difference in meaning: one is being characterized by negation, the other is negation of characterization. Then it explains why answering the non-exhaustive question is not required: because specification is only necessary when the disjunction is exhaustive. Finally, it delves into the meaning of 'does not imply' (lā yaqtaḍī), arguing that the intended implication is by identity or part, not absolute implication, as shown by the example of three implying oddness. It also addresses an objection from Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid about the example of four and man laughing, defending the author's position. The passage ends mid-sentence discussing the correct interpretation of the answer 'neither this nor that'.

Key Terms
الرابطة (al-rabṭa)
السلب (al-salb)
الإيجاب العدولي (al-ījāb al-ʿudūlī)
المعدولتين (al-maʿdūlatayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that any disjunctive question about an essence must be answered with a definite 'yes' or 'no'.
Correction: The passage shows that if the disjunction is not exclusive (i.e., the essence might not imply either alternative), one is not obliged to answer, and if forced, the correct answer is 'neither this nor that'.

Checkpoint
Why are we not obliged to answer when asked 'Is it? or is it not?' using the two modified propositions?
A) Because the question is too vague
B) Because the disjunction is not exclusive
C) Because the answer would be self-contradictory
D) Because the essence always implies both

Key Takeaway
The correct answer to a non-exhaustive disjunctive question about an essence is that it is neither of the two, and such a question does not demand an answer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt pressured to give an answer to a difficult theological question when deep down you knew the options presented did not capture the full truth? How does Sanusi's teaching free you from that pressure? What would it look like to humbly say 'neither this nor that' in such a situation?
?
The lesson shows that a small shift in the placement of a word (the copula) changes the meaning from truth to falsehood. Think of a time when you spoke about Allah or His attributes without careful wording. Can you identify a specific statement you made that might have been logically flawed? What will you do to be more precise in your speech about faith?
?
The passage distinguishes between questions that demand an answer and those that do not. In your own spiritual life, are there questions you keep asking yourself that are not exhaustive—like 'Am I good enough?' or 'Is Allah pleased with me?'—that cannot be answered definitively in this life? How does this lesson's logic help you find peace with not having a closed answer, and instead entrusting the matter to Allah without forcing a resolution?

The Meaning of 'Does Not Imply' and Refutation of Objections
The commentary refutes objections, clarifies the meaning of 'does not imply', and defends the author's position on privative affirmative and implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced meaning of 'does not imply' (la yaqtaḍī) and how it refutes objections about the essence's implications.

Core Idea
Implication can be by identity or part, not only absolute necessity.

This lesson dives into the commentary's detailed discussion of what it means for the essence 'not to imply' something. It refutes the claim of the author of al-Maqāṣid that the essence implies its necessary concomitants, clarifies that the privative affirmative and simple negative are not identical in meaning, and explains that 'does not imply' should be understood as implication by identity or part, not absolute implication. The passage also addresses objections from Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid concerning examples like 'four' and 'man'.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary on al-Maqāṣid
The passage begins by refuting the idea that the essence implies its necessary concomitants. Understand why this claim is false.
This sets up the discussion of 'does not imply'.
2
Examine the argument about privative affirmative
The commentary defends the author's use of 'lā' to express the privative affirmative and refutes the claim that it is equivalent to the simple negative.
Focus on the difference between 'being characterized by negation' and 'negation of characterization'.
3
Understand the meaning of 'does not imply'
The passage explains that 'does not imply' means the essence is not identical to nor a part of the thing in question. It also clarifies that the implication intended is by identity or part, not absolute.
This is a key logical subtlety.
4
Consider the objection and defense
An objection is raised using examples like 'four is even' versus 'man is laughing'. See how the commentary defends the author's position by noting that the 'insofar as' qualification changes the meaning.
The passage ends mid-sentence with a new objection.

What does 'does not imply' (لا يقتضي) mean according to the commentary?
A) The essence has no relationship at all
B) The essence is not identical to nor a part of the thing
C) The essence necessarily avoids the thing
D) The essence implies the opposite

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'does not imply' is nuanced: it means the essence is not identical to nor a part of the thing, and such implication is by identity or part, not absolute.

Resolving the Dilemma of Quiddity's Unity and Multiplicity
Al-Iji presents and responds to an objection about quiddity's unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji resolves the dilemma of quiddity's unity and multiplicity by distinguishing between quiddity in itself and its accidental determinations.

Core Idea
Quiddity in itself is neither one nor many; it is only later qualified by accidents.

In this passage, al-Iji addresses a classic problem: if humanity is one universal, how can it be in two individuals without contradiction? The objector presents a dilemma, and the answer uses a crucial philosophical distinction—the 'min haythu hiya' consideration. This lesson walks through the objection and the subtle response that preserves quiddity's neutrality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the objector's statement. Identify the two horns of the dilemma: if humanity is the same in Zayd and Amr, then one person would be in two places; if different, then humanity is not a shared universal.
Notice that the dilemma relies on assuming that humanity in itself must be either one or many.
2
Understand the answer's strategy
The answer first refuses to engage by pointing out that the dilemma assumes too much. Then it gives the real response: from the respect that quiddity is itself, it is not one nor many, because unity and multiplicity are accidents disregarded in that consideration.
The key phrase is 'min haythu hiya' which means 'from the respect that it is itself'.
3
Reflect on the commentary
Read the commentary to see how the answer is further clarified: the 'min haythu hiya' consideration cuts off all accidents, so quiddity is not subject to the dilemma.
The commentator also notes a subtlety about the wording and another approach by the author of al-Maqasid.

المرصد الثاني - المقصد الأول : في تمييز الماهية عما عداها إذ ليس شيء من الألف واللاألف نفس الماهية ولا داخلاً فيها . ( فإن قيل: الإنسانية التي لزيد ( من حيث إنها إنسانية ( إن كانت هي التي لعمرو كان شخص واحد في آن واحد في (مكانين ومتصفاً بالأوصاف المتقابلة معاً ، ( وإن كانت غيرها، لم تكن الإنسانية أمراً واحداً مشتركاً بين أفراده ( قلنا : ( معنى هذا الكلام أن الإنسانية من حيث هي، إما واحدة مشتركة بين أفراده، وإما متعددة متغايرة فيها، وعلى كل تقدير يلزم محذور، فلا يلزمنا الجواب ؛ لأنها من حيث هي ليست شيئاً مما ذكر، فإن الحيثية المذكورة تقتضي قطع النظر عن جميع العوارض وإن أجبنا قلنا: (هي من حيث هي ليست التي في زيد ولا غيرها وليست التي في عمرو، ولا غيرها؛ لأن وحدتها وتغايرها وكونها في زيد أو عمرو كلها عوارض قطع النظر عنها في هذه الحيثية، ولو وقع بدل قوله : في زيد قولنا في عمرو لكان أظهر. The second observatory - the first aim: distinguishing quiddity from what is other than it. Since nothing of the 'alif' and the 'la-alif' is the quiddity itself nor included in it. (If it is said: the humanity that belongs to Zayd (from the respect that it is humanity) if it is the one that belongs to Amr, then one person would be in two places at once and qualified with opposite attributes together; and if it is other than it, then humanity would not be one thing shared among its individuals. We say: (the meaning of this statement is that humanity from the respect that it is either one shared among its individuals, or multiple differing in it, and on every estimation a contradiction follows, so we are not obliged to answer; because it from the respect that it is is not something of what was mentioned, for the mentioned respect necessitates disregarding all accidents. And if we answer, we say: (it is from the respect that it is is not the one in Zayd nor other than it, and not the one in Amr nor other than it; because its unity and diversity and its being in Zayd or Amr are all accidents that are disregarded in this respect. And if instead of his saying 'in Zayd' he had said 'in Amr' it would be clearer. (Rather both) i.e., being. His saying: (in the sense you know) i.e., humanity from the respect that it is does not necessitate this nor that, but that is after being qualified by existence. His saying: (If it is said etc.) is conjoined to his saying: if we are asked, he brought the fa because the first branching is related to his saying: it is neither existent nor non-existent, and this is related to his saying: nor one nor many; because its property as the commentator said to our statement that humanity from the respect that it is is either one or many, and between their two relatants there is order in mention, so he brought the two branchings likewise, and this is not an objection to what he imagined; for he did not claim previously that humanity is one thing shared among its individuals. His saying: (from the respect that it is humanity) he added the respect by indication of the answer. His saying: (And if instead of his saying etc.) because it is more appropriate for the mentioned question, where he alternated the humanity that belongs to Zayd between being the humanity that belongs to Amr and being other than it, upon the respect, so the answer with the negation entering upon the respect does not match it. I said: we choose the second thing, and we do not admit the lack of match; it would only not match if the purpose was to determine one of the two, but if his opinion was that one of them is established, then no. For the questioner only arranged the conclusion upon the respect based on that opinion, and the answerer alerted by inserting the negative particle on the respect to the error of that opinion, so understand. His saying: (If it is said: humanity etc.) this is an initial sophism about the existence of the absolute shared quiddity, and it is not far that it be objected to his saying: and with multiplicity it is many. His saying: (We said: it is from the respect etc.) and the author of al-Maqasid answered it with another way: that it is the same in reality, other in identity, and it is not impossible for the one not by person to be in multiple places and qualified with opposite attributes, rather it is necessary in the nature of the more general to be like that. And it is not hidden that it is only valid if the respect is not considered, so reflect. Humanity is one shared, and its being multiple differing are two external qualifications from humanity that attach to it after the relation to them, i.e., to unity and multiplicity.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the nature of quiddity (mahiyya), specifically whether humanity (insaniyya) is one and the same in all individuals or multiple. The objector argues that if humanity in Zayd is the same as in Amr, then a single individual would be in two places simultaneously and have contradictory attributes; if different, then humanity would not be a universal shared among its individuals—both impossible. The response uses the crucial distinction of 'min haythu hiya' (from the respect that it is itself): quiddity considered in abstraction from all determinations is neither one nor many, neither existent nor non-existent. The commentary then elaborates on the wording and clarifies that the answer negates the respect itself, not the quiddity. The final sentence summarizes that unity and multiplicity are external accidents that attach to quiddity only after relation to them. The passage thus defends the neutrality of quiddity in itself, a key principle in Islamic metaphysics.

Key Terms
الماهية (quiddity/essence)
الإنسانية (humanity)
الحيثية (respect/consideration)
العوارض (accidents)
الوحدة (unity)
الكثرة (multiplicity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that quiddity in itself must be either one or many, and that the dilemma is unanswerable.
Correction: The passage shows that quiddity in itself is free from all accidents, including unity and multiplicity, so the dilemma does not apply.

Checkpoint
According to al-Iji's response, what is the status of humanity (insaniyya) when considered purely in itself (min haythu hiya)?
A) It is necessarily one.
B) It is necessarily many.
C) It is neither one nor many.
D) It is both one and many.

Key Takeaway
Quiddity considered purely in itself is free from all accidents, including unity and multiplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own identity, do you tend to define yourself by your changing attributes (roles, moods, achievements) rather than your unchanging essence? How does the principle of 'min haythu hiya' challenge you to separate your core self from temporary labels? Write one label you will consciously let go of this week.
?
The objector assumed that quiddity must be either one or many. What similar binary assumptions do you make about your own life or faith? Think of a situation where you forced an 'either/or' choice that might be false. What would it look like to hold the tension without rushing to classify?
?
If quiddity in itself is free from all determinations, what does that imply about your ultimate reality before Allah? In your next prayer, consciously pause to consider your existence as a pure quiddity receiving existence from Him. How does this awareness affect your humility and reliance on Allah?

Logic and Technical Terms
beginner
4 min
The Three Considerations of Quiddity
Al-Iji classifies three considerations of quiddity and clarifies they are not divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ways quiddity is considered in relation to its accidents (mixed, abstract, absolute) and why these are not real divisions but mental considerations.

Core Idea
Quiddity's three considerations are mental, not real divisions.

Al-Iji introduces the second aim of this section: how quiddity is considered with respect to its accidents. He presents three ways: mixed, abstract, and absolute. But he immediately clarifies that this is not a division of quiddity into parts, but rather different perspectives. This lesson establishes the terminology and the key distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three considerations
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Look for the three ways quiddity is considered: mixed (with existence), abstract (without existence), and absolute (unrestricted).
Notice the terms 'bishart shay´', 'bishart la', and 'la bi-shart'.
2
Understand the terminology
Focus on the definitions: mixed is 'conditioned by something', abstract is 'conditioned by not', absolute is 'without condition'. These are the standard terms in kalam.
3
Grasp the clarification
The passage explains that this is not a real division of quiddity. Why? Because absolute quiddity is the divider itself, so dividing it would be dividing a thing into itself and other. It is merely a consideration relative to accidents.
This prevents a common misunderstanding.
4
Reflect on the importance
Consider why this distinction matters: it preserves the unity of quiddity while allowing us to discuss its relationship with accidents. It is a key tool in kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثاني : الماهية إذا أخذت مع قيد زائد ] اعتبارات الماهية بالقياس إلى عوارضها التي ذكر حالها في المقصد الأول، وهي ثلاثة : تقييد الماهية بوجودها، وتقييدها بعدمها وإطلاقها بلا تقييد . فنقول : ( الماهية إذا أخذت مع قيد زائد ) عليها (تسمى مخلوطة، وبشرط شيء ووجودها ) في الخارج ) مما لا مرية (فيه فإن وجود الأشخاص في الخارج بين لا سترة به، وهي عبارة قوله : ( في اعتبارات الماهية) يعني أنه ليس تقسيماً للماهية إلى الاقسام الثلاثة حتى يلزم تقسيم الشيء إلى نفسه وإلى غيره ، لأن الماهية المطلقة عين المقسم بل بيان اعتبارات الماهية بالقياس إلى العوارض وهو الظاهر من عبارة القوم. وفي شرح التجريد أنه تقسيم لحال الماهية إلى الاعتبارات الثلاث، وهو خلاف الظاهر وما قيل إنه تقسيم ما يطلق عليه الماهية فليس بشيء؛ إذ ليس المقصود بيان إطلاقاتها . قوله : (تقييد الماهية فيه إشارة إلى أن المخلوطة والمجردة عبارتان عن الماهية المقيدة بوجود العوارض وبعدمها كما يدل عليه تسميتها بشرط شيء، وبشرط لا، لا عن الماهية مع العوارض ومع عدمها حتى يلزم بطلان الحصر بالماهية المقيدة بها وامتناع وجود المخلوط لأن اعتبارية، ولا عن الماهية المقارنة بها أو بعدمها حتى يلزم صدق المطلقة على المخلوطة من العوارض ما هي قوله: (فإن وجود الأشخاص إلخ) لا يخفى عليك أن الاعتبارات الثلاث إنما هي للماهية بمعنى ما به الشيء هو كلياً كان أو جزئياً، فوجود الجزئيات الحقيقية أعني الأشخاص وجود الماهية المخلوطة إذا اعتبرت تلك الأشخاص مقيدة بالعوارض التي لحقتها بلا ،مرية، ولا حاجة ذلك إلى اعتبار تركيب الشخص من الماهية والتشخص في الخارج. نعم لو كان المراد وجود الماهية الكلية في الخارج، وهو مسألة وجود الكلي الطبيعي في الخارج لاحتيج إلى ذلك. ومن قوله : ( ولو وقع بدل قوله إلخ ) ظاهر كلام السائل يشعر بأن مراده أن الإنسانية التي من حيث هي في زيد هل هي التي في عمرو أم لا ؟ فلو قال المصنف بدل قوله : في زيد في عمرو لربما توهم أن الإنسانية من حيث هي في زيد، فلدفعه من أول الأمر صريحاً قال : ليست التي في زيد، وإن كان ذلك التوهم مندفعاً بقوله : ولا في غيرها . قوله : ( في اعتبارات الماهية إشارة إلى ما صرح به في حواشي المطالع وغيره من أن ما ذكر ليس تقسيماً للماهية إلى الأقسام الثلاثة حتى يكون تقسيماً للشيء إلى نفسه وإلى غيره، بل بيان أن لها اعتبارات ثلاثة بالقياس إلى عوارضها .
The second aim: Quiddity when taken with an additional condition. The considerations of quiddity in relation to its accidents whose state was mentioned in the first aim, and they are three: restricting quiddity by its existence, restricting it by its non-existence, and leaving it unrestricted without restriction. So we say: (Quiddity when taken with an additional condition) upon it (is called mixed, and conditioned by something, and its existence) in the external is beyond doubt, for the existence of individuals in the external is clear without concealment, and it is an expression. His saying: (in the considerations of quiddity) means that it is not a division of quiddity into the three divisions such that a division of a thing into itself and other would follow, because the absolute quiddity is identical to the divider, but rather an exposition of the considerations of quiddity in relation to accidents, and that is apparent from the expression of the scholars. And in the commentary of al-Tajrid it is a division of the state of quiddity into the three considerations, which is contrary to the apparent, and what is said that it is a division of what the quiddity is applied to is nothing; for the purpose is not to expound its applications. His saying: (restricting quiddity) in it indicates that the mixed and the abstract are expressions for the quiddity restricted by the existence of accidents and by their non-existence, as indicated by their naming 'conditioned by something' and 'conditioned by not', not for quiddity together with accidents or together with their non-existence such that the invalidity of the enumeration would follow by the quiddity restricted by them and the impossibility of existence of the mixed because it is mental, nor for quiddity accompanied by them or by their non-existence such that the truth of the absolute on the mixed would follow from the accidents that it is. His saying: (For the existence of individuals etc.) It is not hidden from you that the three considerations are only for quiddity in the sense of that by which a thing is what it is, whether universal or particular. So the existence of real particulars, i.e., individuals, is the existence of the mixed quiddity when those individuals are considered restricted by the accidents that attached to them without doubt, and there is no need for that to consider the composition of the individual from quiddity and individuation in the external. Yes, if the intended meaning was the existence of the universal quiddity in the external, which is the issue of the existence of the natural universal in the external, then that would be needed. And from his saying: (And if instead of his saying etc.) the apparent meaning of the questioner's speech indicates that his intention is whether the humanity that is from the respect that it is in Zayd is the one in Amr or not? So if the author had said instead of his saying 'in Zayd' 'in Amr', perhaps it might be understood that humanity from the respect that it is is in Zayd; so to repel that from the outset he said explicitly: it is not the one in Zayd, even if that understanding is repelled by his saying: 'nor in other than it'. His saying: (in the considerations of quiddity) is an indication to what was stated in the glosses of al-Matali' and others: that what was mentioned is not a division of quiddity into the three divisions such that it would be a division of a thing into itself and other, but rather an exposition that it has three considerations in relation to its accidents.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage defines the three considerations of quiddity (mahiyya) in relation to its accidents. The first aim distinguished quiddity from other things; now the second aim discusses quiddity when taken with an additional condition. The three considerations are: restricting quiddity by existence (making it mixed), restricting it by non-existence (making it abstract), and leaving it unrestricted (making it absolute). The commentary clarifies that this is not a division of quiddity into parts (which would be a division of a thing into itself and other), but rather an exposition of how quiddity can be considered relative to accidents. The mixed is called 'conditioned by something' (bishart shay'), the abstract is 'conditioned by not' (bishart la), and the absolute is 'without condition' (la bi-shart). The commentary also addresses a subtlety: the mixed is not quiddity together with accidents, but quiddity restricted by accidents—a mental consideration. Finally, it notes that the existence of individuals in the external is the existence of the mixed quiddity, and no need to posit composition of individual from quiddity and individuation unless one is discussing the natural universal.

Key Terms
المخلوطة (mixed quiddity)
المجردة (abstract quiddity)
المطلقة (absolute quiddity)
بشرط شيء (conditioned by something)
بشرط لا (conditioned by not)
لا بشرط (without condition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three considerations of quiddity (mixed, abstract, absolute) are real divisions of quiddity into species or parts.
Correction: They are merely mental considerations relative to accidents; the absolute quiddity is the same as the divider, so dividing it would be a logical error.

Checkpoint
What is the term for quiddity when taken with the condition of existence?
A) المجردة (abstract)
B) المخلوطة (mixed)
C) المطلقة (absolute)
D) بشرط لا (conditioned by not)

Key Takeaway
The three considerations of quiddity are mental perspectives relative to accidents, not a division of quiddity into distinct parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that your quiddity (essence) remains the same whether you are in a state of existence or non-existence of something. Where in your life have you confused your circumstances (existence of success or non-existence of failure) with who you truly are? How will you begin separating your identity from your conditions today?
?
The passage warns against dividing quiddity into parts—yet how often do you treat your own nature as split between a 'good you' and a 'bad you'? How would seeing yourself as one essence under different conditions change the way you approach your flaws or weaknesses?
?
When you face loss or disappointment, do you let the absence of something define your entire self? How can the concept of 'absolute quiddity' remind you that your core remains untouched by such conditions? What one concrete action will you take this week to live from that unshakeable self-awareness?

Definition of Mental Attributes and the Objection
Sanusi defines mental attributes and resolves an objection about mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of mental attributes and why mental existence is not classified as a mental attribute.

Core Idea
Mental attributes are what the mind actively makes a condition, not just any mental occurrence.

Sanusi clarifies the meaning of mental attributes by distinguishing between what the mind actively assigns as a condition and what merely occurs in the mind. He then addresses a potential objection: why not call mental existence a mental attribute? This passage shows the precision of kalam terminology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the definition and the objection. Then read the translation to grasp the nuance.
The passage uses parenthetical annotations typical of sharh style.
2
Identify the definition of mental attributes
Find the exact phrase where mental attributes are defined as 'what the mind makes a condition in the thing' and note how the mind actively considers the attribute.
3
Understand the objection and its resolution
Notice the objection about calling mental existence a mental attribute. See how Sanusi responds by allowing the term 'mental concomitant' instead, explaining the difference.
Mental existence occurs in the mind but is not made a condition by the mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(وهذا) الذي فرضناه موجوداً في الذهن ( عرض له في نفس الأمر كونه في الذهن من غير أن يعتبره الذهن عارضاً له ويلاحظه فيه، وبعد وضوح الحق في أن مفهوم العوارض الذهنية ماذا ؟ ( فلا نمنعك أن تسميها ) أي تسمي الأمور العارضة للشيء بحسب نفس الأمر حال كونه موجوداً في الذهن ( باللواحق الذهنية ) ؛ بناء على أن المراد بها ما يلحق الماهية عند قيامها بالذهن، وإن كانت عارضة لها في نفس الأمر لا ما يجعله الذهن قيداً فيها واعتبر عروضه لها . ( وإذا أخذت الماهية من حيث هي هي، مع قطع النظر عن المقارنة ) للعوارض ( والتجرد) عنها (سميت مطلقة وبلا شرط، وهذا أعم من الأوليين. وقد وجدت ) في الخارج إحدى قسميها وهي المخلوطة، ووجود الأخص ) في الخارج ( مستلزم لوجود الأعم فيه، فتكون هي ) أي المطلقة ( أيضاً ( موجودة ) فيه، وذلك ظاهر إذا كان التركيب في الأشخاص خارجياً كما أشرنا إليه .
The Second Observatory - The Second Goal: The quiddity when taken with an additional condition. (Mental) i.e., mental attributes are (what the mind makes a condition in it), i.e., in the thing, by the mind considering an attribute for that thing and observing it for it. (And this) which we assumed existing in the mind (occurs to it in the actual state of affairs that it is in the mind without the mind considering it an attribute for it and observing it in it. And after the truth is clear regarding what the concept of mental attributes is? (then we do not prevent you from calling them) i.e., you call the things that occur to the thing according to the actual state of affairs while it exists in the mind (mental concomitants); based on the fact that what is meant by them is what attaches to the quiddity when it subsists in the mind, even if they are accidental to it in the actual state of affairs, not what the mind makes a condition in it and considers its occurrence to it. (And when the quiddity is taken in itself, disregarding conjunction) with attributes (and abstraction) from them (it is called absolute and unconditioned, and this is more general than the first two. And it has been found) in the external world one of its two divisions, which is the mixed, and the existence of the more specific) in the external (entails the existence of the more general in it, so it) i.e., the absolute (is also (existing) in it, and that is apparent if the composition in individuals is external as we indicated.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage defines mental attributes and clarifies an objection regarding mental existence. It begins by restating the definition: mental attributes are 'what the mind makes a condition in the thing'—that is, the mind actively considers a certain attribute as belonging to the thing and observes it as such. Next, it contrasts this with the case of mental existence itself: we assume something exists in the mind, and it actually occurs in the mind, but the mind does not consider this existence as an attribute or observe it as such. The author then says: after the truth is clear about what mental attributes are, 'we do not prevent you from calling them mental concomitants' (al-lawāḥiq al-dhihniyya). The justification is that what is meant by 'mental concomitants' is whatever attaches to the quiddity when it subsists in the mind, regardless of whether the mind considers it as an attribute. This includes mental existence, even though it is not a mental attribute in the strict sense. Finally, the passage touches on the classification of quiddity: when taken absolutely, disregarding both conjunction with and abstraction from attributes, it is called 'absolute' or 'unconditioned' (muṭlaqa wa bi-lā sharaṭ). This is more general than the mixed (actualized with attributes) and the abstract. Since the mixed exists externally, and the existence of the more specific entails the existence of the more general, the absolute also exists externally, provided the composition in individuals is external as previously indicated.

Key Terms
العوارض الذهنية
الوجود الذهني
اللواحق الذهنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That mental existence is a mental attribute in the sense of something the mind actively considers as an attribute.
Correction: Mental existence occurs in the mind but is not made a condition by the mind; it is a mental concomitant, not a mental attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining characteristic of mental attributes (al-ʿawāriḍ al-dhihniyya)?
A) They occur to the quiddity when it exists in the mind.
B) The mind makes them a condition in the thing and considers them as attributes.
C) They are the same as mental concomitants.
D) They are external attributes of the quiddity.

Key Takeaway
Mental attributes are actively assigned by the mind, while mental existence is a concomitant—it occurs in the mind but is not made a condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that mental attributes are what the mind actively makes a condition. Consider the attributes you assign to Allah—are they based on careful reasoning from revelation, or are you inadvertently imposing mental constructs? Write down one attribute you will re-examine to ensure you are not merely projecting your own mental conditioning.
?
The author resolves an objection by allowing a broader category of 'mental concomitants'. When you encounter a theological disagreement, are you quick to dismiss alternative terminology, or do you seek to understand the underlying reality as the author did? Challenge yourself to engage with one differing perspective this week, focusing on the intended meaning rather than the label.

Classification of Quiddity: Absolute, Mixed, and Abstract
Sanusi classifies quiddity into absolute, mixed, and abstract, and argues for the external existence of the absolute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ways quiddity is considered—with condition, without condition, and absolute—and how the absolute exists externally through the mixed.

Core Idea
The absolute quiddity is broader than mixed and abstract, and exists externally via the mixed.

After clarifying mental attributes, the text turns to a fundamental classification of quiddity itself. When quiddity is taken in itself without any condition, it is called absolute (muṭlaqa). This absolute is more general than the mixed (makhluṭa) and abstract (mujarrada). Sanusi then shows that because the mixed exists externally, the absolute must also exist externally, following the logical principle that the more specific entails the more general.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on quiddity
Read the Arabic sentence starting with 'وإذا أخذت الماهية من حيث هي هي' and its translation. Note the threefold classification introduced.
2
Understand the three categories
Identify: (1) quiddity with a condition – mental attributes, (2) quiddity without condition – abstract, (3) quiddity taken absolutely – the most general. The text focuses on the absolute.
The absolute is called 'unconditioned' (bi-lā sharṭ).
3
Comprehend the existence argument
The passage claims that since the mixed (one specific division) exists externally, and the more specific entails the more general, the absolute also exists externally. Reflect on why this holds if composition in individuals is external.
This argument relies on the logical rule: existence of the specific implies existence of the general.

Source Text

The Refutation of Abstract Individuals for Species
Al-Jurjani refutes the claim of an abstract individual for each species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that there exists an abstract individual for each species is rationally impossible.

Core Idea
An abstract individual cannot exist externally because it would have to admit opposites.

The commentator begins by stating the Platonic claim that each species has an abstract individual. He then refutes it by arguing that an abstract entity cannot exist in external reality, and even if it could, it would be impossible for a concrete individual to be receptive to opposite individualizations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the claim and its proof
The passage starts with a claim: 'There exists from every species an individual abstract from the whole'. Note the brief proof given.
The proof is that a subject for some species cannot be receptive to its opposite.
3
Understand the direct refutation
The commentator immediately rejects the claim because the abstract has no existence in external reality. This is a key principle.
4
Grasp the explanation about quiddity
The commentator explains that only quiddity as such can receive opposites, not a concrete individual. This is illustrated with the human species example.
This shows the impossibility of a concrete individual being receptive to opposite individualizations.

The second observatory - the third aim: There exists from every species an individual abstract from the whole; because that which is a subject for some of them is impossible to be receptive to its opposite. (And you have known that the abstract has no existence in external reality; rather, it is impossible for it to exist in it, so this claim is absolutely false. (And) you also knew that the receptive to opposites is the quiddity insofar as it is itself; for in itself it is capable of being described by each one of them in turn instead of the other, so the quiddity that is accompanied by opposite individualizations. (But as for the existence of an individual of the human quiddity (such that that individual is receptive to Zayd and 'Amr, i.e., to their individualizations, as his statement indicates) it is necessarily false; because it is impossible for a particular individual to be qualified by opposite attributes in absolute humanity at one time. Similarly, if he intends by individual the quiddity restricted by the condition of abstraction, then the conjunction of the abstract with the restrictions from which it was considered abstract is also necessarily false. So it became clear that his proof is insufficient for what His statement: (Because that which is a subject) i.e., in itself. His statement: (So this claim is false etc.) means that his claim is of obvious impossibility, not fit to be heard. So his statement: 'You knew that the abstract has no existence in reality' is an objection that the commentator arranged upon it the falsity of the claim for further clarification. His statement: (For it in itself etc.) The quiddity in itself, since it is nothing but the quiddity, its reception of opposites is by way of substitution. But in the order of existence, it is receptive to them by way of conjunction, because it is existent with existence, non-existent with non-existence, one with unity, many with multiplicity. His statement: (So the human quiddity etc.) The commentator added it to connect his statement: 'But as for the existence of an individual etc.' His statement: (i.e., for their individualizations) so the speech omits the genitive. And he only said 'for their individualizations' even though its reception of a single individualization is also impossible, because the discussion is about reception of opposites. His statement: (Similarly, if he intends etc.) i.e., what was mentioned of it being necessarily false on the supposition that he intends by 'individual' its conventional meaning, i.e., the subject of individualization; even if that is a departure from our topic. And if he intends by it the abstract quiddity, on the basis that it is an individual of the absolute, then it is also necessarily false. Man is capable of non-existence just as he is capable of his other opposite accidents, so the proof, if complete, would necessitate his abstraction from the accidents of existence as well. So how can he judge that he is associated with this accident, namely existence, and abstract from accidents altogether? And it may be said: The apparent meaning of Plato's statement is that he intends to judge the existence of the natural universal. So the meaning of his statement is that the quiddity as such is eternal and everlasting, as indicated by his proof and his statement in the claim: 'capable of opposites', except that he forced the term 'individual' upon the quiddity, supposing that this application is realized in his speech meaning that it is one nature, and 'abstraction' meaning that none of the accidents are the quiddity itself nor a part of it. Then his proof would be valid for his claim, except that there would apply to him what applies to those who affirm the existence of natures. He claimed. And there exists in external reality only particular identities. This that we mentioned applies to him only if his speech is interpreted according to what is apparent from what is transmitted. But if he intends another meaning, such as what some later scholars, namely the Ishrāqī (Suhrawardī), interpreted: that for every species of the spheres, planets, elemental simples, and their compounds, there is an entity from the world of intellects, abstract from matter, subsisting in itself, which manages that species and bestows upon it perfections by its concern and great care inclusive of all its individuals. And it is that which some call the 'Lord of the Species' and is expressed in religious language as in the hadith: the angel of mountains, the angel of seas, the angel of rains, and the like. So that is another discussion unrelated to this topic.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Platonic claim that for every species, there exists an abstract individual. The commentator begins by presenting the claim and then adduces a counter-argument: an abstract entity cannot exist in external reality because it would have to be receptive to opposite individualizations, which is impossible for a concrete individual. He explains that only quiddity as such can receive opposites by substitution, not in actual existence. Then the commentator examines various interpretations: if by 'individual' is meant a concrete particular, the claim is false; if it means the quiddity abstracted from matter, it is also false because abstraction from matter would preclude existence. He then considers an alternative interpretation by Suhrawardī, who takes the 'abstract individual' to mean a separate intellect (the Lord of the Species). The commentator dismisses this as a different discussion not relevant to the present inquiry. Throughout, the commentator follows the order of the original proof and its refutation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a universal concept can exist as a concrete individual in the external world.
Correction: The universal (quiddity) exists only in the mind; a concrete individual cannot be receptive to opposite instantiations.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject the claim that there exists an abstract individual for each species?
A) Because the proof relies on a false premise

C) Because the proof contradicts religious texts
D) Because the claim is not found in Aristotle

Key Takeaway
The claim of an abstract individual for each species is false because an abstract entity cannot exist externally and a concrete individual cannot be receptive to opposites.

?

?

Alternative Interpretations of the Abstract Individual
Al-Jurjani examines alternative interpretations of the abstract individual and dismisses them as irrelevant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative interpretation of the abstract individual as a separate intellect (Lord of the Species) and why it is considered a different discussion.

Core Idea
The only viable interpretation of the abstract individual is the Suhrawardian Lord of the Species, but that is unrelated to the present inquiry.

After refuting the obvious interpretation, the commentator considers alternative readings. He examines the possibility that Plato meant the natural universal, and then discusses Suhrawardi's interpretation of a separate intellect managing each species. He ultimately dismisses the latter as a different topic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure of alternatives.
2
Understand the second refutation
The first alternative (individual as quiddity restricted by abstraction) is shown to be self-contradictory.
The conjunction of the abstract with restrictions undermines the abstraction.
3
Consider the natural universal interpretation
The commentator suggests Plato might have meant the natural universal, but this leads to a different set of problems.
This interpretation is not the focus of the present discussion.
4
Learn about Suhrawardi's Lord of the Species
The final interpretation is that the abstract individual is a separate intellect managing the species. The commentator says this is a different discussion.
This is an important metaphysical concept but outside the current debate.

الماهية المقيدة بقيد التجرد، فإن اقتران المجرد بالقيود التي اعتبر تجريده عنها ضروري البطلان أيضاً ، فظهر أن دليله غير واف بما قوله : ( لأن ما يكون معروضاً) أي في نفسه . قوله : (فهذا المدعى باطل إلخ) يعني أن دعواه بديهي الاستحالة لا يليق أن يسمع، فقوله : علمت أن المجرد لا وجود له في الحقيقة معارضة رتب الشارح قدس سره عليها بطلان الدعوى للاستظهار. قوله : (فإنها في حد ذاتها إلخ الماهية في حد ذاتها لما لم تكن إلا الماهية كان قبولها للمتقابلات بطريق البدلية، وأما في مرتبة الوجود فهي قابلة لها بطريق الاجتماع؛ لكونها مع الوجود موجودة، ومع العدم معدومة، ومع الوحدة واحدة، ومع الكثرة كثيرة . قوله : ( فالماهية الإنسانية إلخ زاده الشارح قدس سره ليرتبط قوله : وأما وجود فرد إلخ . قوله : ( أي لتشخصهما فالكلام على حذف المضاف، وإنما قال : لتشخصهما مع أن قبوله لتشخص واحد أيضاً محال؛ لأن الكلام في قبول المتقابلات . قوله : ( وكذا إن أراد إلخ) أي ما ذكر من كونه ضروري البطلان على تقدير إرادته بالفرد معناه المتعارف أي معروض التشخص ؛ وإن كان ذلك خروجاً عما نحن فيه، وإن أراد به الماهية المجردة بناء على أنه فرد للمطلقة فهو أيضاً ضروري البطلان . نميع الإنسان قابل للغدم كما هو قابل لسائر عوارضه المتقابلة فيوجب الدليل على تقدير تمامه تجرده عن عوارض الوجود أيضاً، فكيف يحكم بمقارنته لهذا العارض أعني الوجود وتجرده عن العوارض البتة. وقد يقال : الظاهر من كلام أفلاطون أن مراده الحكم بوجود الكلي الطبيعي، فمعنى كلامه أن الماهية من حيث هي أزلية أبدية بقرينة دليله وقوله في المدعى : قابل للمتقابلات إلا أنه تمحل في إطلاق الفرد على الماهية على تقدير تحقق هذا الإطلاق في كلامه بمعنى أنها طبيعة واحدة، وفي التجرد بمعنى أن شيئاً من العوارض ليست نفس الماهية ولا جزءاً منها، وحينئذ يكون دليله وارداً على مدعاه غايته أنه يرد عليه ما ورد على القائلين بوجود الطبائع. ادعاه . ( ولا يوجد في الخارج إلا الهويات الجزئية (هذا الذي ذكرناه إنما يرد عليه ( إن حمل كلامه على ما هو ظاهر المنقول عنه، وإن عني به معنى آخر مثل ما أوله به بعض المتأخرين وهو صاحب الإشراق (من أن لكل نوع من الأفلاك والكواكب والبسائط العنصرية ومركباتها ( أمراً) من عالم العقول (مجرداً) عن المادة قائماً بذاتها ( يدبره ) أي يدبر ذلك النوع، ويفيض عليه كمالاته بشأنه عنايةً ويعتني عظيمةً شاملةً لجميع أفراده، (وهو الذي يسميه ذلك البعض ( رب النوع ) ويعبر عنه في لسان الشرع كما ورد في الحديث بملك الجبال وملك البحار، وملك الأمطار ونحوها ، ( فذلك بحث آخر لا تعلق له بهذا المقام .
The second observatory - the third aim: There exists from every species an individual abstract from the whole; because that which is a subject for some of them is impossible to be receptive to its opposite. (And you have known that the abstract has no existence in external reality; rather, it is impossible for it to exist in it, so this claim is absolutely false. (And) you also knew that the receptive to opposites is the quiddity insofar as it is itself; for in itself it is capable of being described by each one of them in turn instead of the other, so the quiddity that is accompanied by opposite individualizations. (But as for the existence of an individual of the human quiddity (such that that individual is receptive to Zayd and 'Amr, i.e., to their individualizations, as his statement indicates) it is necessarily false; because it is impossible for a particular individual to be qualified by opposite attributes in absolute humanity at one time. Similarly, if he intends by individual the quiddity restricted by the condition of abstraction, then the conjunction of the abstract with the restrictions from which it was considered abstract is also necessarily false. So it became clear that his proof is insufficient for what His statement: (Because that which is a subject) i.e., in itself. His statement: (So this claim is false etc.) means that his claim is of obvious impossibility, not fit to be heard. So his statement: 'You knew that the abstract has no existence in reality' is an objection that the commentator arranged upon it the falsity of the claim for further clarification. His statement: (For it in itself etc.) The quiddity in itself, since it is nothing but the quiddity, its reception of opposites is by way of substitution. But in the order of existence, it is receptive to them by way of conjunction, because it is existent with existence, non-existent with non-existence, one with unity, many with multiplicity. His statement: (So the human quiddity etc.) The commentator added it to connect his statement: 'But as for the existence of an individual etc.' His statement: (i.e., for their individualizations) so the speech omits the genitive. And he only said 'for their individualizations' even though its reception of a single individualization is also impossible, because the discussion is about reception of opposites. His statement: (Similarly, if he intends etc.) i.e., what was mentioned of it being necessarily false on the supposition that he intends by 'individual' its conventional meaning, i.e., the subject of individualization; even if that is a departure from our topic. And if he intends by it the abstract quiddity, on the basis that it is an individual of the absolute, then it is also necessarily false. Man is capable of non-existence just as he is capable of his other opposite accidents, so the proof, if complete, would necessitate his abstraction from the accidents of existence as well. So how can he judge that he is associated with this accident, namely existence, and abstract from accidents altogether? And it may be said: The apparent meaning of Plato's statement is that he intends to judge the existence of the natural universal. So the meaning of his statement is that the quiddity as such is eternal and everlasting, as indicated by his proof and his statement in the claim: 'capable of opposites', except that he forced the term 'individual' upon the quiddity, supposing that this application is realized in his speech meaning that it is one nature, and 'abstraction' meaning that none of the accidents are the quiddity itself nor a part of it. Then his proof would be valid for his claim, except that there would apply to him what applies to those who affirm the existence of natures. He claimed. And there exists in external reality only particular identities. This that we mentioned applies to him only if his speech is interpreted according to what is apparent from what is transmitted. But if he intends another meaning, such as what some later scholars, namely the Ishrāqī (Suhrawardī), interpreted: that for every species of the spheres, planets, elemental simples, and their compounds, there is an entity from the world of intellects, abstract from matter, subsisting in itself, which manages that species and bestows upon it perfections by its concern and great care inclusive of all its individuals. And it is that which some call the 'Lord of the Species' and is expressed in religious language as in the hadith: the angel of mountains, the angel of seas, the angel of rains, and the like. So that is another discussion unrelated to this topic.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Platonic claim that for every species, there exists an abstract individual. The commentator begins by presenting the claim and then adduces a counter-argument: an abstract entity cannot exist in external reality because it would have to be receptive to opposite individualizations, which is impossible for a concrete individual. He explains that only quiddity as such can receive opposites by substitution, not in actual existence. Then the commentator examines various interpretations: if by 'individual' is meant a concrete particular, the claim is false; if it means the quiddity abstracted from matter, it is also false because abstraction from matter would preclude existence. He then considers an alternative interpretation by Suhrawardī, who takes the 'abstract individual' to mean a separate intellect (the Lord of the Species). The commentator dismisses this as a different discussion not relevant to the present inquiry. Throughout, the commentator follows the order of the original proof and its refutation.

Key Terms
الكلّي الطبيعي
رب النوع
المجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the abstract individual claim can be saved by interpreting it as a separate intellect (Lord of the Species).
Correction: The Lord of the Species is a separate concept and does not support the original claim of an abstract individual existing in external reality.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say about Suhrawardi's interpretation of the abstract individual?
A) It is the correct interpretation of Plato's claim
B) It is a necessary belief in Islam
C) It is a different discussion unrelated to the present topic
D) It contradicts the principle of quiddity

Key Takeaway
The alternative interpretations do not support the original claim of an abstract individual existing in external reality; the Suhrawardian Lord of the Species is a separate metaphysical concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a difficult claim about faith or existence, do you ever force an interpretation to make it fit your preconceptions, rather than accepting its apparent meaning? What might that cost you?
?
The commentator dismisses the Lord of the Species interpretation as 'another discussion'. Are there beliefs or arguments you hold that you have never critically examined because you considered them 'another topic'? What would it take to examine them now?
?
This lesson shows how tangential debates can distract from the core issue. Is there a matter of aqeedah where you have gotten lost in secondary details, avoiding the real question of whether your belief aligns with revelation? How can you refocus?

Classification of Quiddities into Simple and Composite
Al-Jurjani defines simple and composite quiddities and argues for the necessity of simples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of simple and composite quiddities and the argument that composites must end in simples to avoid an infinite regress of parts.

Core Idea
Composite quiddities require simple building blocks or we face an infinite regress.

This lesson introduces a foundational classification in metaphysics: quiddities are either simple or composite. Al-Jurjani defines these terms and provides a crucial argument that any composite must end in something simple, otherwise we face an infinite regress of parts. This argument is key to understanding the structure of reality in the Islamic philosophical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a heading and then the main definitions.
2
Define simple and composite
Identify the definition of a simple quiddity (does not coalesce from several actually assembled things) and a composite quiddity (coalesces from several assembled things).
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا تلتئم من عدة أمور تجتمع' for simple.
3
Understand the argument for composites ending in simples
Follow the logical argument: every composite must have parts each of which is one reality; otherwise there would be an infinite regress of parts. This forces the existence of simple quiddities.
The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum: infinite regress is impossible.
4
Clarify what 'simple' means
Simple does not mean having no parts whatsoever, but having no actual parts. Potential parts (like a line having potential halves) are allowed.
This is a common point of confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الرابع: الماهية إما بسيطة أو مركبة ] ( الماهية إما بسيطة لا تلتئم من عدة أمور تجتمع، أو مركبة تقابلها فهي التي تلتئم من عدة أمور مجتمعة ( وينتهي المركب إلى البسيط) إذ لا بد أن يكون في المركب أمور كل واحد منها حقيقة واحدة، وإلا لكان مركباً من أمور لا نهاية لها لا مرة واحدة بل مراراً غير متناهية ومع ذلك فلا بد من وجود البسيط ، قوله : ( من أن كل إلخ فمعنى كلامه أنه يوجد لأجل كل نوع من الأجسام البسيطة والمركبة فرد في نفسه؛ لا من ذلك النوع مجرد عن المادة قابل أي مقبل من قبل بمعنى أقبل على ما في القاموس ؛ للمتقابلات أي للأشخاص المتقابلة لا للعوارض المتقابلة. قوله : ( بهذا المقام أي مقام البحث من الماهية المجردة، فلا يرد أنه أيضاً من مباحث الماهية من حيث أن لها رباً . قوله : (إما بسيطة) قدمها مع أن مفهومها عدمي لتعلق حكم المركبة به . قوله : (تجتمع) ذكره لإفادة أن المعتبر في البسيط أن لا يكون أجزاء لها بالفعل، ولا يعتبر انتفاء الأجزاء بالقوة، فإن الخط والسطح والجسم التعليمي بسائط مع أن لها جزءاً بالقوة . قوله : (إذ لا بد أن يكون في المركب (أمور أي أمر أن كل واحد منهما متصف بالوحدة بالفعل بلا واسطة أو بواسطة أو بوسائط . قوله : ( وإلا لكان إلخ) أي وإن لم يكن كل واحد من تلك الأمور واحداً بالفعل كان بعضها مركباً من أمور غير متناهية بالفعل. قوله : (بل مراراً غير متناهية لأنه إذا فرض جزء منها بحيث لا ينتهي إلى البسيط كان ذلك الجزء مركباً من أمور غير متناهية وكذلك جزء الجزء وهو ما يبقى بعد إسقاط واحد من تلك قوله: (مثل ما أوله به إلخ) هذا التأويل مستبعد جداً، فإن رب كل نوع ليس فرداً منه ولا يعرض له المتقابلات، وإنما يدبره بنوع تعلق بأفراده.
[The fourth aim: The quiddity is either simple or composite.] (The quiddity is either simple, not coalescing from several things that come together, or composite, its opposite, which coalesces from several things that come together. And the composite ends in the simple, because there must be in the composite things each of which is one reality; otherwise it would be composite from infinite things not just once but infinitely many times, and despite that the existence of the simple is necessary. His statement: (that for every etc.) So the meaning of his speech is that for every species of simple and composite bodies there exists an individual in itself, not of that species, abstract from matter, receptive (i.e., from the direction, meaning oriented, as in al-Qāmūs) to opposites, i.e., to opposite individuals, not to opposite accidents. His statement: (to this topic) i.e., the topic of inquiry about the abstract quiddity; so it is not objected that it is also among the inquiries of the quiddity insofar as it has a lord. His statement: (either simple) He gave it priority even though its concept is privative because the ruling of the composite depends on it. His statement: (come together) He mentioned it to indicate that what is considered in the simple is that it has no actual parts; the absence of potential parts is not considered, for line, surface, and the mathematical body are simples even though they have a part potentially. His statement: (because there must be in the composite things) i.e., that each of them is qualified by actual unity, whether without intermediary or with an intermediary or intermediaries. His statement: (otherwise it would be etc.) i.e., if each of those things were not actually one, then some of them would be composite from actually infinite things. His statement: (but infinitely many times) because if a part of them is assumed such that it does not end in the simple, that part would be composite from infinite things, and likewise the part of the part, which remains after dropping one of those. His statement: (such as what he interpreted etc.) This interpretation is very far-fetched, for the lord of a species is not an individual of it, nor does it incur opposites; rather, it manages it by a kind of relation to its individuals.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a classification of quiddities. It first defines simple and composite quiddities: simple does not consist of actually combined parts, while composite does. It then argues that composites must ultimately be composed of simples, because otherwise there would be an infinite regress of parts, each of which must be actually one. The commentator explains that the simple quiddity is prioritized even though its concept is privative. He clarifies that simplicity means absence of actual parts, not potential parts (e.g., line, surface, space are simple despite having potential divisibility). The commentator also critiques an interpretation of the Lord of Species, stating it is unrelated to the topic of quiddities.

Key Terms
الماهية البسيطة
الماهية المركبة
الجزء بالفعل
الجزء بالقوة
الوحدة بالفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the definition of a simple quiddity according to the text?
A) That which has no parts at all
B) That which does not coalesce from several actually assembled things
C) That which is indivisible in every way
D) That which exists only in the mind

Key Takeaway
Composites must be composed of simples; otherwise an infinite regress of parts would occur, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever examined the foundations of your own beliefs to see if they rest on simple, unshakable truths, or are they just composites of inherited opinions? What would it take for you to seek those simple truths?
?
Al-Jurjani argues that complexity cannot go on forever—there must be something simple at the core. In your spiritual life, are you building on a simple, direct relationship with Allah, or have you buried it under endless layers of secondary matters?
?
If you knew that your faith had a simple, irreducible essence, would you be willing to let go of everything that is merely composite—customs, preferences, distractions—to cling to that essence? What is stopping you?

Logic and Technical Terms
