I said: He meant by 'quiddity' the quiddity composed of them only, as is evident from context, and also it has been established that the constituent of 'animal' is one of them, and they are mentioned together in its definition due to ignorance of which is prior and constituent, so the need for consideration is established in any case. His statement: ('Like 'white body'; the theologians do not speak of the surface, and then it is clear that 'body' is absolutely more general than 'white', and that is obvious. Then 'body' is its constituent, i.e., determiner and actualizer, because 'white' has no actuality by itself but only within a type like 'body'. His statement: ('Rather the opposite is the case') and it is of two divisions: a division where the general takes the role of the described and the specific takes the role of the attribute, and a division the opposite; the author gave an example of the first and the commentator of the second. His statement: ('It is the constituent of 'animal'') i.e., the determiner and actualizer for it, not the one entering into its constitution as is well-known in the meaning of 'constituent', and hence it is said that the differentia of a species is a constituent for it and a divider of the genus.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the relations between parts (اجزاء) in terms of generality and specificity (عموم وخصوص). The author explains that when two things do not apply to all individuals of each other but coincide in some cases, they have a relationship of generality and specificity. This is divided into two types: absolute (مطلقاً) and from a certain aspect (من وجه). In the absolute type, either the general constitutes the specific (يقوم العام الخاص) or vice versa. The first case (general constitutes specific) occurs in mental quiddities like 'white body': the intellect considers the two as one quiddity composed of general and specific. The second case (specific constitutes general) is exemplified by 'rational animal': here 'rational' (the specific) is the differentia that constitutes the genus 'animal', and similarly 'existing substance' where 'existence' is an attribute of the substance. The author then clarifies that 'rational' is not an attribute but a constituent. The commentary explains the reasoning: in the first case, the general is self-constituting and the specific subsists by it; in the second case, the specific is the actualizer. The passage also discusses the case of generality from a certain aspect, giving the example 'white animal' as a mental quiddity because the real quiddity has no intellectual composition except from genus and differentia or coextensive terms. The commentary adds that the examples given by the author and the commentator illustrate the two subcases: in one the general functions as the subject (موصوف) and the specific as the attribute, and in the other the roles are reversed. The key point is that the term 'constituent' (مقوم) here means determiner and actualizer, not component in the usual sense.

Key Terms
مقوم
ماهية اعتبارية
ماهية حقيقية
الجسم الأبيض
الحيوان الناطق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'constituent' (مقوم) always means a component that enters into the essence.
Correction: In this context, 'constituent' means determiner and actualizer, not necessarily an essential component. For example, 'rational' is called a constituent of 'animal' because it determines and actualizes the species, not because it is a component in the sense of being part of the definition's basic constituents.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what does the term 'constituent' (مقوم) mean in the example of 'rational animal'?
A) A component that enters into the essence
B) A determiner and actualizer
C) An attribute of the genus
D) The genus itself

Key Takeaway
In this context, 'constituent' means determiner and actualizer, not an essential component, and the reverse situation has two subcases: general as subject or specific as subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own understanding of yourself, do you confuse the essential attributes that define you (like your humanity) with accidental attributes (like your skin color or status)? How does this lesson's distinction between 'determiner' and 'component' help you see which attributes truly constitute your identity before Allah?
?
The passage shows that we may not know which of two aspects is prior in constituting something. In your life, are there matters where your ignorance of the 'constituent' element leads you to treat both as equally important, thus missing the true determiner? Will you commit to seeking the deeper root of your actions and beliefs?
?
The theologians in this passage do not speak of 'surface' (sath) because it is not a distinct entity. Are there 'surfaces' in your religious practice—outward actions that you treat as if they have independent reality—while neglecting the deeper reality that actualizes them? What is one surface you will discard today to focus on the essence?

Logic and Technical Terms

Classification of Distinct Parts by Relation to Causes
Classifies considerations of a thing with respect to its causes, effects, and unrelated items.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how distinct parts are classified based on their relation to causes, effects, or neither, and how the objection of redundancy is resolved through focusing on relations rather than entities.

Core Idea
Distinct parts are categorized by their association with causes, effects, or neither.

This passage presents a logical classification of distinct parts according to their relation to causes and effects. It addresses a potential redundancy objection by clarifying that composition is with the relation, not the cause or effect itself. The first category is then subdivided into efficient, receptive, and formal causes, each illustrated with an example.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three-fold classification
Read the initial classification: a distinct part can be considered with a cause, an effect, or neither. Note the parentheses indicating three categories.
This is the overarching division.
2
Examine the objection and its answer
The objection claims redundancy: considering a thing with its cause would imply considering it with its effect as well. The answer clarifies that composition is with the relation to the cause, not the cause itself, so no redundancy occurs.
This resolution is crucial for the logical soundness of the division.
3
Explore the subdivision of the first category
The first category (considered with a cause) is further divided into three: with the efficient cause (e.g., 'giving'), with the receptive cause (e.g., 'snubness'), and with the formal cause (e.g., 'snub-nosed').
Each example involves a specific relation.
4
Reflect on the examples
Identify how 'giving', 'snubness', and 'snub-nosed' illustrate the different types of cause and the role of the relation in their definitions.
Consider the difference between including the relation alone versus including both relation and relatum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمتنع أن يكون بين أجزائها عموم من وجه ( وأما المتباينة : فإما أن يعتبر الشيء مع علة من علله الأربع ( أو ) مع (معلول) له ( أو ) مع ( ما ليس علة ولا معلولاً) بالقياس إليه فإن قلت تركب الشيء مع علته يستلزم تركب الشيء الذي هو مع معلوله، ففي التقسيم استدراك قلت : معنى تركب الشيء مع علته، أن العلة تلك يعتبر ذلك الشيء من حيث عرضت له الإضافة إلى تلك العلة، ومعنى تركب الشيء مع معلوله أن يعتبر من حيث عرضت له الإضافة إلى ذلك المعلول، فلا استدراك أصلاً . ( والأول ) ، وهو المعتبر بالقياس إلى العلة : ( إما ) معتبر ( مع الفاعل، نحو العطاء ) فإنه اسم لفائدة اعتبرت إضافتها إلى الفاعل ( أو ) مع القابل نحو الفطوسة، وهي الذي في الأنف اعتبر فيها الشيء بالإضافة إلى قابله ( أو ) مع (الصورة نحو الأفطس ) قوله : ( وأما المتباينة فإما أن يعتبر إلخ) أي فحالها اعتبار الشيء إلى آخره . • التقعير قوله : ( أن يعتبر ذلك الشيء إلخ بأن يعتبر الإضافة داخلة دون المضاف إليه، كما في العطاء، أو يعتبر كلاهما داخلة كما في الأفطس، أو يعتبر المضاف إليه فقط نحو السرير فإنه عبارة عن الخشب والهيئة والإضافة التي بينهما غير داخلة فيه ولظهوره لم يورد له مثالاً، وحينئذ يكون معنى تركب الشيء مع ما ليس علة ولا معلولاً أن يكون فيه تركيب مع أمر ليس علة اعتبرت الإضافة إليه، ومعلولاً كذلك، سواء لم يكن علة ولا معلولاً كما في العشرة، أو كان علة ومعلولاً ، لكن لم يعتبر كونه مضافاً إليه كما في الجسم، فإنه مركب من الهيولى والصورة، وكل واحدة منهما علة للأخرى، لكن لم يعتبر فيه كون إحداهما مضافاً إلى الأخرى. وبما حررنا ظهر كون الحصر بين الأقسام عقلياً، وانطبقت الأمثلة كلها مع الممثل له، واندفع الشكوك التي عرضت للناظرين. قوله : (من علله الأربع المراد من العلل الأربع الفاعل والغاية والمادة والصورة، لكن ليس المراد بالمادة ما هو داخل في قوام المعلول حتى يرد الاعتراض على التمثيل بالفطوسة، لما سيجيء من أن المحل بالقياس إلى الحال يشبه المادة مشابهة تامة فهي معدودة في عدادها، وقس عليه حال الصورة . هو قوله : قلت : معنى تركب الشيء إلخ) ليس مراده أن الأخذ مطلقاً مع الشيء معنی الأخذ بالقياس إليه وإلا لم ينحصر في الأقسام المذكورة مع عدم استقامته في بعض الأمثلة، بل الأخذ مراده تعمیم مع الشيء الأخذ مع الإضافة إليه والأخذ مع ذاته، وهذا العموم يكفي في دفع الاستدراك كما لا يخفى، ففي العبارة مسامحة قوله: (نحو العطاء) قال في حواشي التجريد : الداخل في مفهوم العطاء هو الإضافة إلى الفاعل ،دونه، لكن لا تتعقل الإضافة بدون تعقله وقس على ذلك كثيراً من الأمثلة. واعلم أن ما سوى أجزاء العشرة ليس مثالاً للشيء المعتبر مع غيره، كما يتبادر من كلامه بل للماهية المركبة
It is impossible that there be between its parts generality from a certain aspect. (As for the distinct: then either the thing is considered with a cause from among its four causes, or with an effect of it, or with what is neither cause nor effect relative to it). For if you say: the composition of a thing with its cause necessitates the composition of a thing that is with its effect, so there is a redundancy in the division. I say: the meaning of the composition of a thing with its cause is that the thing is considered in respect of the relation that has occurred to it to that cause, and the meaning of the composition of a thing with its effect is that it is considered in respect of the relation that has occurred to it to that effect, so there is no redundancy at all. (And the first), which is the one considered relative to a cause: (either) considered (with the efficient cause, like 'giving'), for it is a name for a benefit considered with its relation to the agent, (or) with the receptive cause like 'snubness', which is a thing in the nose considered with its relation to its recipient, (or) with the formal cause like 'snub-nosed'. His statement: ('As for the distinct: then either it is considered, etc.') i.e., its situation is considering the thing, etc. ... His statement: ('that that thing is considered, etc.') by considering the relation as included without the relatum, as in 'giving', or considering both included as in 'snub-nosed', or considering only the relatum like 'bed', for it is an expression for the wood and the shape and the relation between them is not included in it, and due to its obviousness he did not provide an example. Then the meaning of composition of a thing with what is neither cause nor effect is that it has composition with an entity that is not a cause considered with its relation to it, and not an effect either, whether it is neither cause nor effect like 'ten', or it is both cause and effect but the relation to it is not considered, like the body, for it is composed of matter and form, each of which is a cause for the other, but the fact that one is related to the other is not considered. With what we have explained, it becomes clear that the enumeration among the divisions is rational, and all the examples correspond to what they represent, and the doubts that occurred to observers are dispelled. His statement: ('from among its four causes') he means the four causes: agent, end, matter, and form, but the term 'matter' here does not mean what enters into the constitution of the effect such that the objection to the example of 'snubness' would arise, because it will come that the substrate relative to the accident completely resembles matter, so it is counted among its kind, and analogously for form. His statement: ('I say: the meaning of composition of a thing, etc.') he does not mean that taking 'with a thing' absolutely means taking in relation to it, for then it would not be limited to the mentioned divisions and would not be consistent in some examples, but rather 'taking' here means generalization: taking the thing together with the relation to it and taking its essence, and this generality suffices to rebut the redundancy, as is not hidden, so there is a license in the expression. His statement: ('like 'giving') in the glosses on al-Tajrid said: what enters into the concept of 'giving' is the relation to the agent, without the agent, but the relation cannot be conceived without conceiving it, and analogize many examples. And know that other than the parts of 'ten' is not an example of the thing considered with something else, as his words might suggest, but rather of the composite quiddity.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage discusses the division of parts that are distinct (متباينة) from each other. It begins by stating that it is impossible for there to be generality from a certain aspect between such parts. Then it presents a classification: when a thing is considered together with something else, that something else is either a cause, an effect, or neither cause nor effect relative to it. An objection is raised: if we consider a thing with its cause, that would imply considering it with its effect as well, making the division redundant. The answer clarifies that the composition is not with the cause itself but with the relation to the cause; similarly for the effect, so no redundancy occurs. The first type (consideration with a cause) is further divided into three: with the efficient cause (e.g., 'giving' which involves a relation to the agent), with the receptive cause (e.g., 'snubness' which involves a relation to the nose as substrate), and with the formal cause (e.g., 'snub-nosed'). The commentary explains that the inclusion of the relation can be in three ways: the relation included without the relatum (as in 'giving'), both relation and relatum included (as in 'snub-nosed'), or only the relatum included (as in 'bed' which includes the wood and shape but not the relation). Then it discusses the case of consideration with what is neither cause nor effect: this includes things like 'ten' (which is purely numerical) or things that are causes and effects of each other but not considered as such, like a body composed of matter and form. The passage concludes that this rational division is exhaustive and that the examples match. Additional commentary clarifies that the term 'matter' in this context includes accidental substrates that resemble matter, and that the phrasing in the objection should be understood as a generalization to include either the relation or the essence, not just the relation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Composition of a thing with its cause means the cause becomes part of the thing.
Correction: It means the thing is considered in respect of its relation to the cause, not the cause itself.

Checkpoint

D) The cause is an attribute of the thing

Key Takeaway
Distinct parts are classified by their relational context, not by their inherent components.

Logic and Technical Terms

Classification of the Disparate and Refutation of Redundancy
Classification of the disparate into three types and refutation of an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how disparate things are classified into three types based on causal relations, and why the objection of redundancy is refuted through the concept of relational addition (إضافة).

Core Idea
The three types of disparate relations are distinguished by the direction of the addition, not by the entities themselves.

This passage from al-Mawaqif presents a precise logical classification of how distinct things can be considered in relation to each other. It then anticipates and refutes a potential objection, demonstrating the rigorous method of kalam. You will see how careful attention to relational perspectives avoids redundancy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the structure of classification and refutation come through.
2
Identify the three types
Note the three ways a thing can be considered with something else: with a cause, with an effect, or with neither cause nor effect.
3
Understand the objection
Why might someone think the first two types are redundant? Because cause and effect are correlatives—if you combine with one, you seem to combine with the other.
4
Study the refutation
The author uses the concept of الإضافة (addition/relation) to show that the addition is to the cause in one and to the effect in the other, making them distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المتباينة : فإما أن يعتبر الشيء مع علة من علله الأربع ( أو ) مع (معلول) له ( أو ) مع ( ما ليس علة ولا معلولاً) بالقياس إليه فإن قلت تركب الشيء مع علته يستلزم تركب الشيء الذي هو مع معلوله، ففي التقسيم استدراك قلت : معنى تركب الشيء مع علته، أن العلة تلك يعتبر ذلك الشيء من حيث عرضت له الإضافة إلى تلك العلة، ومعنى تركب الشيء مع معلوله أن يعتبر من حيث عرضت له الإضافة إلى ذلك المعلول، فلا استدراك أصلاً .
As for the disparate (المتباينة), it is either that the thing is considered with a cause from among its four causes, or with an effect of it, or with what is neither a cause nor an effect in relation to it. For if you say: the combination of the thing with its cause necessitates the combination of the thing that is with its effect, so in the division there is redundancy; I say: the meaning of the combination of the thing with its cause is that the thing is considered from the perspective of the addition (الإضافة) to that cause, and the meaning of the combination of the thing with its effect is that it is considered from the perspective of the addition to that effect, so there is no redundancy at all.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of things that are mutually disparate (متباينة) – i.e., distinct in essence – and how they can be considered in relation to other things. It divides such consideration into three types: (1) considering the thing together with one of its four causes, (2) considering it together with one of its effects, or (3) considering it together with something that is neither a cause nor an effect. The author then anticipates an objection: someone might argue that the first two types are actually the same because if you combine a thing with its cause, that combination implies a combination with its effect (since the cause produces an effect). Hence the division would be redundant. The author refutes this by clarifying the perspective of the 'addition' (إضافة): in the first case, the addition is to the cause; in the second, to the effect. They are distinct relational properties, so the division is valid. This logical precision is typical of kalam reasoning.

Key Terms
متباينة
علة
معلول
إضافة
استدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that combining a thing with its cause and combining it with its effect are the same relation because cause and effect are inseparably linked.
Correction: The author explains that the relation (addition) is considered to the cause in one and to the effect in the other; they are distinct relational properties, so no redundancy occurs.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say there is no redundancy (استدراك) in the division of the disparate?
A) Because the four causes are distinct from each other
B) Because the addition (إضافة) is considered to the cause in one and to the effect in the other
C) Because the third type includes both similar and different parts
D) Because the objector misunderstood the term 'cause'

Key Takeaway
The classification of disparate things into three types is maintained by distinguishing the relational addition to cause, effect, or neither, thereby avoiding redundancy.

?

The Third Type: Consideration with Neither Cause nor Effect
Third type with subdivisions: similar quiddity and different quiddity distinct mentally

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third rational consideration: the coexistence of things that are neither cause nor effect, and its two subdivisions based on quiddity.

Core Idea
When things are neither cause nor effect, their coexistence is judged by quiddity similarity or difference.

After covering the first two types (cause and effect relationships), the author turns to the third: considering a thing with something that is neither its cause nor its effect. This category requires a different kind of analysis. The passage introduces two subdivisions: when the things are similar in quiddity and when they are different, and further refines the latter by how they are distinct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the definition of the third type, then the two subdivisions.
The passage ends abruptly; the missing word is understood as 'sensation'.
2
Identify the two main subdivisions
Look for the phrases 'similar in quiddity' and 'different in quiddity'. Note the examples given for each: units of ten for similar, and body composed of matter and form for different.
3
Understand the distinction within 'different in quiddity'
The passage specifies that the different-quiddity type can be distinct either mentally or sensorily. The example given (matter and form) is mentally distinct. Reflect on why matter and form are not sensorily separable.
This emphasizes the role of intellect in distinguishing realities that are unified in sensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والثالث ) : وهو الذي اعتبر مع ما ليس علة ولا معلولاً، إما متشابهة) في الماهية (نحو أجزاء العشرة) الوحدات المتوافقة الحقيقة ( أو متخالفة في الماهية وهي (إما) متمايزة (عقلاً) لا حساً، ( كالجسم المركب من الهيولى والصورة، فإن أجزاءه متخالفة متمايزة في العقل دون
And the third is what is considered with what is neither a cause nor an effect. It is either similar in quiddity (ماهية), like the parts of ten – the units of uniform reality – or different in quiddity, and it is either mentally distinct (عقلاً) not sensorily, like the body composed of matter (هيولى) and form (صورة), for its parts are different and distinct in the mind without [sensation].
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage explains the third category: considering a thing along with something that is neither its cause nor its effect. This category is subdivided into two: (1) when the parts are similar in quiddity, such as the units that compose the number ten – they are all alike in being units, only numerically distinguished; (2) when the parts are different in quiddity, and here they are further divided into those that are distinct only mentally (عقلاً) but not sensorily (حساً), like the composition of a physical body from prime matter (هيولى) and substantial form (صورة). In reality, matter and form are inseparable, but the intellect distinguishes them as two principles. 'Without sensation' means they cannot be perceived separately by the senses.

Key Terms
ماهية (quiddity)
متشابهة (similar)
متخالفة (different)
هيولى (prime matter)
صورة (form)
عقل (intellect)
حس (sensation)
أجزاء العشرة (parts of ten)
جسم (body)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things are different in quiddity, they must be sensorily distinct.
Correction: The example of matter and form shows that some differences are only mentally distinct, not sensorily perceived.

Checkpoint
What example is given for parts that are different in quiddity but distinct only mentally?
A) أجزاء العشرة (parts of ten)
B) الخالق (Creator)
C) الجسم المركب من الهيولى والصورة (body composed of matter and form)
D) الأفطس (flat-nosed)

Key Takeaway
The third rational consideration (neither cause nor effect) covers coexistence of things, subdivided by quiddity similarity (units of ten) and difference (e.g., matter and form), with the latter further refined by how difference is perceived—mentally or sensorily.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, you often encounter things that seem inseparable (like the form and matter of your own body). When you reflect on your actions, do you distinguish between the physical act and its spiritual intention? How does this intellectual discipline help you avoid conflating outward deeds with inward sincerity? What specific step will you take today to mentally separate your intention from the action before you perform it?
?
The passage teaches that some distinctions are only in the mind, not in sensory reality. Think of a belief you hold about Allah or the unseen that you cannot perceive with your senses. How does this lesson reassure you that intellectual certainty is valid even without sensory proof? Where in your faith are you tempted to demand sensory evidence? Will you commit today to trusting what your intellect affirms, even when your senses object?
?
The units of ten are identical in quiddity but distinct in number. Consider the Muslims you know: they share the same essential belief (الإيمان), yet each is distinct. Do you treat fellow believers as essentially identical or do you let superficial differences (like ethnicity or school of thought) obscure their shared faith? What relationship will you repair or build this week based on this truth?

Existential and Real vs. Relative Parts
Division of parts into existential vs. non-existential and then real vs. relative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between existential and non-existential parts, and between real and relative parts, and why 'existential' here refers to conceptual content, not external existence.

Core Idea
Existential parts are those whose definitions contain no privation.

This passage introduces the second major division of parts in kalam ontology. Before we can analyze composition, we need to know whether the parts are defined by presence or absence. The author distinguishes existential parts (concepts without negation) from others, and then further subdivides existential parts into real and relative. Pay attention to the example of man composed of spirit and body as a 'mental composition' – this highlights the conceptual nature of the division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first a division into two, then a further division of one branch.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the next page continues the division.
2
Identify the two main divisions
Find where the author says 'إما وجودية' (either existential) and 'أولا تكون كذلك' (or not). Understand the criterion: existential parts have no negation in their concept.
The term 'وجودية' here is technical – it doesn't mean 'existing' in the external world.
3
Examine the sub‑division and examples
The first main group (existential) is further divided into 'حقيقية' (real) and its opposite (relative). The example of body from matter and form is given as a real composition; man from spirit and body is called a 'mental composition' (تركيب اعتباري). Note why the latter is called mental.
The 'mental composition' indicates a conceptual unity, not a physical one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقسيم ( الثاني : أنها ) أي الأجزاء (إما وجودية ) بأسرها بمعنى أنه لا يكون في مفهوماتها سلب، (أولا) تكون كذلك، (و) القسم الأول : إما حقيقية ) أي غير إضافية ) كما مر)، من الجسم المركب من الهيولى والصورة، والإنسان المركب تركيباً اعتباريا من الروح والجسد، ( أو ) ... قوله: (والإنسان المركب تركيباً اعتباريا من الروح والجسد وإنما قال تركيباً اعتبارياً؛ لأن هي
The second division: that [i.e. the parts] are either existential entirely, meaning that there is no negation in their concepts, or they are not like that. And the first division is either real, i.e. non-relative, as has passed, like the body composed of matter and form, and man composed by a mental composition from spirit and body, or ... His saying: (and man composed by a mental composition from spirit and body) and he only said 'mental composition' because it is ...
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage introduces the second major division of parts: they are either existential (وجودية) in the sense that their concepts contain no negation, or they are not (i.e., they involve some negation). Within the existential category, parts are further divided into real (حقيقية) meaning non-relative, as previously exemplified by body from matter and form, and man composed by a mental composition from spirit and body. The commentator notes that the composition of man is called 'mental' (اعتباري) because it is not a physical composition but a conceptual one. The passage is cut off at the end of the page, so further elaboration follows in subsequent pages.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking 'existential' means the part exists as an independent entity in the external world.
Correction: In this division, 'existential' means the concept of the part contains no privation, not that it has external existence. For example, 'blindness' is a real quality but its concept involves negation (lack of sight), so it would be non‑existential.

Checkpoint

B) Parts whose concepts contain no negation
C) Parts that are composed of matter and form
D) Parts that are relative

Key Takeaway
The division of parts into existential and non‑existential is about conceptual content, not mode of existence; existential parts have no privation in their definition.

?

Interpreting 'Outside' as Sensible or Mental
Defines the two interpretations of 'outside' in part division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of 'outside' in the division of parts: one as sensible and one as opposed to the mind.

Core Idea
Parts can be divided by whether they are distinct in the senses or in the mind.

The text begins by defining the division of parts according to sense perception. It presents two interpretations of the term 'outside': one meaning sensible, like limbs of the body, and another meaning opposed to the mind, like matter and form. This lesson clarifies these two meanings and their examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two meanings
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice the two ways 'outside' is interpreted: as sensible and as opposed to the mind.
Pay attention to the examples given for each.
2
Examine the examples
The passage gives examples like justice (composite of virtues), limbs, matter, form, color, shape, and black and white. Note which interpretation each example belongs to.
Some examples are sensorily distinct, others are conceptual.
3
Reflect on the importance
Ask yourself why it matters to distinguish these two meanings. Consider how it affects the classification of parts in theological and philosophical discussions.
The commentary explains the reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الخامس : في تقسيم الأجزاء
الحس، وكالعدالة المركبة من الحكمة والعفة والشجاعة أو خارجاً) أي حساً كأعضاء البدن، وعلى هذا ففي قوله : (نحو) الإنسان المركب من النفس والبدن ) نظر، فإن النفس الناطقة والبدن لا يتمايزان حسا، وإن أريد بالخارج ما يقابل الذهن، كانت الهيولى والصورة من الأجزاء الخارجية دون العقلية، (و) نحو الخلقة المركبة من اللون والشكل المتمايزين في الحس، فإن الهيئات الشكلية محسوسة تبعاً، ونحو البلقة المركبة من السواد والبياض المحسوسين بالذات .
قوله : ( خارجاً أي حساً فسر إلخارج بالحس متابعة لما ذكره الإمام في المباحث المشرقية وغيره من قسمة الأجزاء إلى المعقولة والمحسوسة. قوله : ( فإن النفس الناطقة إلخ ) لأن التمايز الحسي يقتضي أن يكون كل منهما محسوساً نقل عنه ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأنه يكفي في التمايز الحسي كون البدن محسوساً دون النفس الناطقة انتهى .
The second observation - the fifth objective: On the division of parts. Sense, and like justice composed of wisdom, temperance, and courage; or externally, i.e., sensibly, like the limbs of the body. And upon this, there is consideration in his statement: 'like man composed of soul and body,' because the rational soul and the body are not sensorily distinct. And if by 'external' is meant that which opposes the mind, then matter and form are among the external parts, not the mental ones. And like the creation composed of color and shape which are sensorily distinct, for the shape-forms are sensibly perceived secondarily, and like the piebald composed of black and white which are sensorily perceived essentially. His statement: 'Externally, i.e., sensibly' — he interpreted 'external' as sense in accordance with what Imam mentioned in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya and others regarding the division of parts into intellectual and sensible. His statement: 'For the rational soul, etc.' — because sensory distinction requires that each of them be sensible, as reported from him, and it can be answered that it suffices for sensory distinction that the body be sensible without the rational soul. End.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage defines the division of parts based on whether they are distinct in sense or in the mind. It begins by giving examples: justice composed of virtues is a part in a sense, and external parts like limbs. Then it notes an objection: if man is composed of soul and body, they are not sensorily distinct, so how can man be an example? The passage provides an alternative interpretation: if 'external' means opposed to the mind, then form and matter are external parts, not mental. Then the commentary explains that the author interprets 'external' as sense, following the Imam, and answers the objection by saying that sensory distinction only requires one part to be sensible, like the body, even if the soul is not.

Key Terms
خارج
حس
تمايز حسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'outside' always means sensible or perceptible by the senses.
Correction: The passage shows that 'outside' can also mean that which is opposed to the mind, referring to external realities like matter and form.

Checkpoint
According to the first interpretation, what does 'outside' mean?
A) Distinct in the mind
B) Sensible
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The term 'outside' in the division of parts has two meanings: sensible and mental, and the examples clarify how parts are categorized.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider what is 'outside' of you—your body, your possessions—do you only focus on what you can sense? Or do you acknowledge realities like your soul and character that are not physically detectable? How does this affect your priorities in daily life? Will you take one concrete step today to attend to your inner reality?
?
The passage points out that justice, though not a physical object, is a composite of virtues. Are you aware of the invisible parts of your own character—like your wisdom, temperance, and courage? Do you actively cultivate them as much as your outer achievements? What virtue will you deliberately practice this week?
?
The rational soul and body are not sensorily distinct. Do you live as if your soul is as real as your body? What would change in your actions if you truly believed that your soul's health is the most important 'external' reality? Will you spend five minutes today reflecting on your soul's state?

Resolving the Objection on Man's Composition
Addresses the objection about man's composition and provides a resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the rational soul and body are not sensorily distinct, and how the commentator resolves it.

Core Idea
Sensory distinction requires only one part to be sensible, not both.

The previous lesson noted an objection regarding the example of man composed of soul and body, since the soul is not sensible. The commentator now addresses this objection, explaining that 'outside' is interpreted as sensible in line with other scholars, and he provides a resolution: it suffices for one component to be sensible.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's explanation
Read the Arabic passage and the translation. Notice the commentator's interpretation of 'outside' as sensible, following the Imam.
This sets the context for the objection.
2
Understand the objection
The objection states that sensory distinction requires both parts to be sensible. Since the rational soul is not sensible, the example of man as a composite of soul and body would not fit the category of 'external in sense.'
The phrase 'نقل عنه' indicates this is reported from the Imam or another authority.
3
Learn the resolution
The resolution is that it suffices for one part to be sensible. Therefore, because the body is sensible, the composite can still be considered externally distinct in sense, even though the soul is not sensible.
This preserves the example of man as valid.

Three Types of Existential Parts
Enumerates the second division of parts into wholly existential or not, and details three subtypes of existential parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of existential parts in logical composition: real, relative, and mixed.

Core Idea
Existential parts come in three kinds: real, relative, or mixed.

Before diving into the details of logical parts, it is important to see how philosophers categorize them. This passage presents the second major division: parts are either wholly existential or not. Among existential parts, there are three subtypes that determine how concepts are composed. As you read, notice the examples given for each type.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the classification.
Focus on the distinction between 'wholly existential' and 'not wholly existential'.
2
Identify the two main divisions
Notice that the passage first divides parts into existential or not. Then the existential parts are further divided into three subtypes: real, relative, and mixed.
The three subtypes are the core of this lesson.
3
Reflect on the examples
Examine the examples: body (real), 'the nearer' (relative), bed (mixed). Why is each classified that way?
Ask yourself: what makes 'the nearer' relative? What makes the bed mixed?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقسيم ( الثاني : أنها ) أي الأجزاء (إما وجودية ) بأسرها بمعنى أنه لا يكون في مفهوماتها سلب، (أولا) تكون كذلك، (و) القسم الأول : إما حقيقية ) أي غير إضافية ) كما مر)، من الجسم المركب من الهيولى والصورة، والإنسان المركب تركيباً اعتباريا من الروح والجسد، ( أو إضافية نحو الأقرب ) فإن مفهومه مركب من القرب والزيادة فيه، وكلاهما إضافيان ( أو ممتزجة ) من الحقيقية والإضافية (نحو السرير) فإنه مركب من القطع الخشبية وهي موجودات ،حقيقية، ومن ترتيب مخصوص فيما بينها باعتباره يتحصل السرير، وأنه أمر نسبي لا يستقل بالمعقولية .
The second division: that they (i.e., the parts) are either wholly existential, meaning that there is no negation in their concepts, or they are not like that. And the first division is either real (i.e., non-relative, as previously), like the body composed of matter and form, and man composed conceptually from spirit and body; or relative, like 'the nearer' for its concept is composed of nearness and increase in it, and both are relative; or mixed from real and relative, like the bed, for it is composed of wooden pieces which are real existents, and a specific arrangement among them by which the bed is achieved, and that is a relational matter not independent in conceptualization.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents the second division of parts: they are either all existential (i.e., no negation in their meanings) or not. The first kind is subdivided into three: real parts (like body from matter and form, or man from spirit and body conceptually), relative parts (like 'the nearer' which involves the relative concepts of nearness and increase), and mixed parts (like the bed, which combines real wooden pieces with a relative arrangement). The author provides examples to clarify each subtype.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'real' part is sometimes confused with 'existential', but the author distinguishes them by noting that real parts are non-relative, while existential includes all three subtypes.
Correction: Real parts are a subtype of existential parts that are not relative. Existential parts also include relative and mixed parts.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Existential parts are classified into real, relative, and mixed, based on whether they contain relative elements or not.

Parts Including Negation and the Scope of Divisions
Describes parts that include negation and states that these divisions apply to essence in general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that concepts can combine existential and non-existential components, and that the divisions of parts apply to essences in general, whether real or conceptual.

Core Idea
Concepts can include negation, but pure nonexistence is unintelligible without relation to existence.

In this passage, Jurjani explains the second kind of parts—those that are not wholly existential. He uses 'al-qadim' as an example, showing that its definition includes both an existential component and a negation. He then clarifies that these divisions apply to essence (mahiyya) universally, but if we restrict to real essences, their parts are entirely existential.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the example
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the example of 'al-qadim' (the eternal) and understand why its concept is composed of both existential and non-existential parts.
Notice that 'al-qadim' is existent but has no beginning.
2
Understand the unintelligibility of pure nonexistence
Jurjani states that pure nonexistence is not considered because it is unintelligible. Reflect on why nonexistents are only conceived in relation to existents.
This guards against the misconception that pure negation can stand alone as a concept.
3
Grasp the scope of the divisions
The divisions (existential vs. non-existential parts) apply to essence (mahiyya) absolutely, whether real or conceptual. However, if we consider only real essences, their parts are necessarily existential.
This shows that the classification is universal but restricted when dealing with real essences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والثاني) وهو ما لا تكون بأسرها وجودية (نحو القديم، فإنه موجود لا أول له فقد يتركب مفهومه من وجودي وعدمي، ولم يتعرض لما هو عدمي محض ؛ لأنه غير معقول، فإن العدمات لا تعقل إلا مضافة إلى الوجودات، فيكون المعنى الوجودي ملحوظاً هناك قطعاً . ( واعلم أن هذه الأقسام المذكورة في هذين التقسيمين إنما هي (في الماهية) على الإطلاق ، ( أعم من أن تكون ) ماهية ( حقيقية أو اعتبارية. وأما إذا اعتبرنا الماهية الحقيقية فلا تكون أجزاؤها إلا موجودة فتكون وجودية قطعاً، فلا يتأتى فيها
And the second (type) is that which is not wholly existential, like 'the eternal,' for it is an existent with no beginning, so its concept is composed of an existential and a non-existential. And he did not mention what is pure nonexistence, because it is unintelligible, for non-existents are only conceived as added to existents, so the existential meaning is necessarily observed there. And know that these mentioned divisions in these two divisions are only concerning the essence (al-mahiyya) absolutely, whether it is a real essence or a conceptual one. But if we consider the real essence, then its parts are only existent, so they are necessarily existential, and it does not apply to it
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage discusses the second kind of parts: those that are not wholly existential. The example is 'al-qadim' (the eternal), which is existent but has no beginning, so its concept includes both an existential component (existence) and a negative one (no beginning). The author notes that he did not give an example of pure nonexistence because it is unintelligible; nonexistents are only conceived in relation to existents, so the existential aspect is always present. Then the author states that all these divisions apply to essence (mahiyya) in general, whether real or conceptual. However, if we consider only real essences, their parts are purely existential, so the division into existential and non-existential does not apply to real essences (since they cannot include nonexistence).

Key Terms
قديم
عدم
عدمي
ماهية
حقيقية
اعتبارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that pure nonexistence can be a part of a concept or can be understood on its own.
Correction: The author argues that pure nonexistence is unintelligible; non-existents are only conceived as additional to existents, so the existential meaning is always present.

Checkpoint
Why is 'al-qadim' an example of parts that are not wholly existential?
A) Because it is eternal.
B) Because its concept includes a negative component (no beginning).
C) Because it is pure nonexistence.
D) Because it is an existent.

Key Takeaway
Concepts can include negative components, but pure nonexistence is never a concept on its own; the divisions of parts apply to all essences generally, but not to real essences specifically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a negative thought you often have about yourself (e.g., 'I lack patience'). According to this lesson, such a negative concept is only intelligible in relation to a positive one. What is the positive existence that your thought is really pointing to, and how can focusing on that existence change your self-perception?
?
The author says 'non-existents are only conceived as added to existents.' Think of a time you felt a deep sense of absence or emptiness. Did you realize that that feeling was actually dependent on something existing that you desired? How does this truth call you to gratitude even in loss?
?
Consider the division between real and conceptual essences. The real essence has only existential parts. When you look at your own existence, do you tend to focus on the conceptual parts (what you lack) rather than the real, existential parts (what Allah has given you)? What would it mean for you today to shift your attention to the existential reality of Allah's blessings?

Classification of Quiddities by Existence and Non-existence
The author classifies quiddities by existence and non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second division of quiddities according to existence and non-existence, its distinction from the first division, and the condition regarding relations.

Core Idea
Quiddities are divided by existence, not by reality or relation.

After the first division of quiddities by reality and relation, the author introduces a second division based on existence and non-existence. This classification, however, comes with an important condition: it holds only if relations are not considered as external existents. The passage also touches on the impossibility of certain relations between parts of a real quiddity, such as general-special and equality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms and the structure: first the division, then the condition, then the examples of impossible relations.
2
Identify the division criteria
The passage contrasts two divisions: one by existence/non-existence and one by reality/relation. Understand that the author favors the former under the stated condition.
The condition is crucial: 'if relations are not made among external existents.'
3
Examine the example of impossible relations
The author mentions that certain relations, like general-special and equality, are impossible between parts of a real quiddity. Reflect on why this is significant for defining quiddities.
This relates to the composition of a real quiddity from parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقسيم الثاني باعتبار الوجودية والعدمية، ولا باعتبار الحقيقية والإضافية إذا لم تجعل الإضافات من الموجودات الخارجية والنسبة بينها أي بين أجزاء الماهية الحقيقية قد تمتنع على بعض الوجوه المذكورة في التقسيم الأول، كالعموم من وجه على المشهور وكالمساواة على ما قيل من امتناع تركب الماهية الحقيقية الواحدة وحدة حقيقية من أمرين متساويين.
The second division is according to existence and non-existence, and not according to reality and relation, if relations are not made among external existents. And the relation between them, i.e., between the parts of the real quiddity, may be impossible in some of the ways mentioned in the first division, such as general-special in the well-known view, and equality according to what has been said regarding the impossibility of a single real quiddity being composed of two equal things in a real unity.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of quiddities. The author divides them into existential and non-existent, as opposed to the division into real and relational. The qualification 'if relations are not made among external existents' indicates that this division is valid only if relations are not considered separate external entities. Then the author discusses the relation between parts of a real quiddity, noting that certain relations, like common and special (general-special) and equality, are impossible in some cases, based on the impossibility of a single real quiddity being composed of two equal parts. This is a technical classification.

Correction: The second division uses a different criterion (existence vs. non-existence) and only applies when relations are not treated as external existents.

Key Takeaway
The second division of quiddities is based on existence and non-existence, with the condition that relations are not considered external existents, and certain relations between parts are impossible.

?

The Objection and Initial Modal Response
Objection and initial answer distinguishing necessary from possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial objection and the key response that distinguishes between impossibility in itself and possibility in the context of the debate on whether quiddities are made.

Core Idea
The absence of making is possible, not impossible, otherwise making would be necessary.

This passage introduces an objection to the proof that quiddities are not made. The objector suggests that the absence of making might be impossible, leading to a contradiction. The author answers by clarifying that absence is not impossible in itself; if it were, making would be necessary. This sets the stage for a deeper logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of modal terms like 'impossible in itself' and 'possible in itself'.
2
Identify the modal distinction
The answer distinguishes between 'impossible in itself' and 'possible in itself'. Understand why this distinction is crucial for the argument.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If making is possible in itself, why doesn't its absence entail impossibility? The passage explains that possibility implies contingency.
This connects to the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأورد عليه أنه يجوز أن يكون عدم الجعل محالاً مستلزماً للمحال والجواب : أن عدم الجعل ليس ممتنعاً بالذات، وإلا لكان الجعل واجباً بالذات، فنقول : لو كان الجعل ممكناً بالذات أمكن عدمه نظراً إلى ذاته، ولو أمكن في ذاته لما حكمنا باستلزامه المحال. ثم قال: محال بديهة. والجواب : أنا لا نسلم استحالته، فإن المعدوم في الخارج دائماً مسلوب عن نفسه دائماً، فإذا ارتفع الجعل في وقت أو دائماً، ارتفعت الإنسانية كذلك، فيصدق قولنا : ليست الإنسانية إنسانية في الخارج ويكون صدق السالبة الخارجية؛ لعدم الموضوع في الخارج، وليس ذلك بمحال، وإنما المحال هو الإيجاب المعدول. وحاصله : أن عند عدمه أي عدم جعل الجاعل ترتفع الماهية الإنسانية عن الخارج رأساً وبالكلية، فلا يصدق عليها حكم إيجابي، بل يصدق سلب جميع الأشياء حتى سلب نفسها عنها بحسب الخارج؛ لا أنها تتقرر في نفس الأمر. ثم قال: قوله فإذا ارتفع إلخ يعني أن السند أعني قوله : فإن المعدوم إلى آخره مذكور بطريق التنظير والمقصود أنه إذا كان المعدوم في الخارج مسلوباً عن نفسه، فكذلك الماهيات إذا ارتفع جعلها، أي لم يتعلق الجعل بها ارتفعت بالمرة أي لم تكن ذواتها، فيصح سلبها عنها، فلا يرد أن الكلام في الماهيات في حد ذواتها لا في الماهيات المعدومة، فالسند المذكور لا يصلح للسندية، والمراد بالخارج هاهنا نفس الأمر. قوله : ( ويكون صدق السالبة الخارجية لم يرد بالخارجية ما هو المتعارف بينهم، إذ ليس الحكم هاهنا على الأفراد فضلاً عن المحققة، بل ما يكون الخارج فيها ظرف الحكم، وكما أن السالبة تكون صادقة كذلك الموجبة السالبة المحمول؛ إذ لا إيجاب فيها حقيقة، بل مجرد اعتبار، فلا يرد أنه إذا صدق السالبة المذكورة صدق الموجبة السالبة المحمول لتلازمهما، لكن صدقها محال؛ لأنه يلزم إثبات سلب الشيء للشيء. قوله : ( لعدم الموضوع في الخارج أي بارتفاع الموضوع، أعني مفهوم الإنسانية بالمرة في نفس الأمر، كما أن صدق السالبة الخارجية المتعارفة يكون بعدم أفراد الموضوع في الخارج. قوله : ( ويكون صدق السالبة الخارجية إلخ) قيل : فيه بحث؛ لأن القضية القائلة : الإنسانية إنسانية، وكذا في كل ماهية قضية ذهنية فسالبتها لو صدقت لعدم الموضوع، صدقت لعدمه في الذهن لا لعدمه في الخارج كما زعمه وبالجملة القائل بمجعولية الماهية يقول : إن كون الإنسانية إنسانية في نفس الأمر بجعل الجاعل، لا أن كونها إنسانية في الخارج به؛ إذ ماله حينئذ إلى مجعولية الهوية؛ لأن الإنسان في الخارج عين الهوية ولا كلام فيه والنافي بمجعوليتها يقول : لو كانت الإنسانية مجعولة لم تكن الإنسانية إنسانية في نفس الأمر عند عدم الجعل، فحينئذ لا يتجه الجواب بأن صدق السالبة لعدم وجود الموضوع في الخارج، فتأمل.
And it was objected against it that it is possible that the absence of making is impossible, entailing impossibility. The answer: the absence of making is not impossible in itself, otherwise making would be necessary in itself. We say: if making is possible in itself, its absence is possible with respect to itself, and if it is possible in itself, we would not judge that it entails impossibility. Then he said: obvious impossibility. The answer: we do not concede its impossibility, for the non-existent in the external is always negated of itself always. So if making is removed at a time or always, humanity is also removed, so our statement 'humanity is not humanity in the external' is true, and the external negative judgment is true due to the absence of the subject in the external, and that is not impossible. The impossible is the negative positive (i.e., affirmative negative). Its summary: when it is absent, i.e., the absence of the maker's making, the quiddity of humanity is completely removed from the external, so no positive judgment is true of it, rather the negation of all things, even the negation of itself with respect to the external, is true; it is not that it is established in reality. Then he said: his statement 'when it is removed, etc.' means that the proof, i.e., his statement 'for the non-existent' etc., is mentioned by way of analogy. The intention is that just as the non-existent in the external is negated of itself, so likewise quiddities when their making is removed, i.e., when making is not attached to them, are completely removed, i.e., they do not have their essence, so their negation of themselves is valid. So it is not objected that the discussion is about quiddities in themselves, not about non-existent quiddities. Thus the mentioned proof is not suitable as a proof, and 'external' here means reality. His statement: 'and the truth of the external negative judgment' does not intend by 'external' what is customary among them, for the judgment here is not about individuals, let alone verified ones, rather the external is the locus of the judgment. And just as the negative is true, similarly the affirmative with negative predicate (i.e., the subject is non-existent) is true, for there is no affirmation in reality, but mere consideration. So it is not objected that if the mentioned negative is true, then the affirmative with negative predicate is true because they are equivalent, but its truth is impossible because it would require establishing a negation of a thing of itself. His statement: 'due to the absence of the subject in the external' means due to the removal of the subject, i.e., the concept of humanity completely in reality, just as the truth of the customary external negative judgment occurs due to the absence of individuals of the subject in the external. His statement: 'and the truth of the external negative judgment, etc.' It was said: there is discussion in this; because the proposition 'humanity is humanity', and similarly for every quiddity, is a mental proposition, so its negative, if true due to absence of subject, would be true due to its absence in the mind, not due to its absence in the external as he claimed. In summary, the proponent of quiddities being made says that the fact that humanity is humanity in reality is by the maker's making, not that it is humanity in the external thereby. For what belongs to it then is the made-ness of identity, because man in the external is identical to identity, and there is no discussion about that. The denier of their being made says: if humanity were made, then humanity would not be humanity in reality when making is absent. Then the answer that the truth of the negative is due to the absence of the subject in the external is not appropriate. So reflect.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the proof that quiddities are not made, followed by a detailed answer. The objector suggests that the absence of making might be impossible, which would entail impossibility. The answer begins by distinguishing necessary from possible: the absence of making is not impossible in itself, otherwise making would be necessary. If making is possible, its absence is possible. Then the answer addresses the claim that negating a thing of itself is impossible: when the maker's making is absent, the quiddity is completely absent from the external world, so the negative judgment 'humanity is not humanity' is true due to lack of subject, not as a contradiction. The commentator then clarifies the meaning of 'external' in this context, distinguishing it from the customary usage. There is further discussion about the truth of negative judgments and the identity of quiddities. The passage concludes with a note that the answer may not be sufficient if the quiddity is considered in itself rather than in the external. This is a complex dialectical exchange central to the debate on whether quiddities are made.

Key Terms
عدم الجعل
محال
ممتنع بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The absence of making is impossible.
Correction: The absence is not impossible in itself; otherwise making would be necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, if making is possible in itself, then its absence is:
A) impossible
B) necessary
C) possible
D) contradictory

Key Takeaway
The absence of making is not impossible in itself; it is only impossible if making is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own stubborn habits, do you treat them as 'impossible in themselves' to change, or as 'possible in themselves' but difficult? What would it mean to truly believe that even your deepest flaws are not impossible by their own nature?
?
If you knew that your refusal to repent from a sin is not impossible in itself, but merely contingent on your choice, would you still delay repentance? What is holding you back if not impossibility?
?
The passage distinguishes between 'impossible in itself' and 'possible but rejected due to entailment'. Are there beliefs you hold as 'necessary' that are actually only 'possible'? How would your life change if you admitted that?

Negative Self-Predication and External Absence
Answer using external negative judgments to defend negating quiddity of itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the answer uses the concept of external negative judgments to show that negating a quiddity of itself is not impossible when the quiddity is absent from the external world.

Core Idea
Negating a thing of itself is possible if the thing is non-existent in the external.

This part of the answer addresses the claim that saying 'humanity is not humanity' is impossible. The author explains that when making is absent, the quiddity is completely removed from the external world, so the negative judgment is true due to the absence of the subject, not due to contradiction. This clarifies the logical status of such statements.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection of obvious impossibility
The objector claims that negating a quiddity of itself is obviously impossible. Read the passage and note the author's rejection of this claim.
2
Understand the analogy with non-existent things
The author draws an analogy with a non-existent thing in the external, which is always negated of itself. See how this supports the argument.
This is key to the answer.
3
Distinguish between external negative and negative positive
The author distinguishes between 'external negative' (saliba kharijiyya) and 'negative positive' (ijab ma'dul). Understand why the former is possible and the latter is not.
4
Grasp the summary and analogy
Read the summary: when making is absent, the quiddity is not established in reality. Note that the proof is by analogy with non-existent things.

When you encounter a teaching that seems to contradict itself, do you immediately assume it is impossible, or do you pause to consider whether the contradiction might be due to a difference in perspective (like internal vs. external)? How can you train yourself to avoid hasty judgments?
?
The author defends saying 'humanity is not humanity' when humanity is absent. Are there beliefs you hold dear that would lose their truth if the subject were absent from reality? What does that say about the foundation of your certainty?
?
Reflect on a time you insisted something was 'obviously impossible' in matters of faith. After studying this lesson, are you open to the possibility that your judgment was based on an incomplete understanding? What would it take for you to revise such a conviction?

Clarifying 'External' and Final Discussion
Clarification of 'external' and final objection summary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the subtle distinction between external as locus of judgment and external as world of individuals, and the final objection that the answer may not apply when considering quiddities in themselves.

Core Idea
The 'external' in this discussion is reality (nafs al-amr), not the world of physical individuals.

This final part of the answer clarifies the technical meaning of 'external' and addresses a potential objection that the negative judgment would only be true if the quiddity is absent from the mind, not the external. The commentator then summarizes the positions of the proponent and denier of quiddities being made, highlighting the tension.

Guided Path
1
Understand the clarification of 'external'
The author clarifies that 'external' in this context is the locus of judgment, not the customary world of individuals. Read carefully.
2
Note the equivalence of judgments
The external negative and affirmative with negative predicate are equivalent in this context. Understand why the latter is not impossible.
3
Consider the objection about mental propositions
Some object that 'humanity is humanity' is a mental proposition, so its negative would be true due to mental absence. Reflect on this objection.
4
Reflect on the summary of positions
Read the summary contrasting the proponent and denier of quiddities being made. See why the answer might not be fully satisfactory.
The passage ends with 'fa-ta'ammal' (so reflect), indicating further contemplation is needed.

And it was objected against it that it is possible that the absence of making is impossible, entailing impossibility. The answer: the absence of making is not impossible in itself, otherwise making would be necessary in itself. We say: if making is possible in itself, its absence is possible with respect to itself, and if it is possible in itself, we would not judge that it entails impossibility. Then he said: obvious impossibility. The answer: we do not concede its impossibility, for the non-existent in the external is always negated of itself always. So if making is removed at a time or always, humanity is also removed, so our statement 'humanity is not humanity in the external' is true, and the external negative judgment is true due to the absence of the subject in the external, and that is not impossible. The impossible is the negative positive (i.e., affirmative negative). Its summary: when it is absent, i.e., the absence of the maker's making, the quiddity of humanity is completely removed from the external, so no positive judgment is true of it, rather the negation of all things, even the negation of itself with respect to the external, is true; it is not that it is established in reality. Then he said: his statement 'when it is removed, etc.' means that the proof, i.e., his statement 'for the non-existent' etc., is mentioned by way of analogy. The intention is that just as the non-existent in the external is negated of itself, so likewise quiddities when their making is removed, i.e., when making is not attached to them, are completely removed, i.e., they do not have their essence, so their negation of themselves is valid. So it is not objected that the discussion is about quiddities in themselves, not about non-existent quiddities. Thus the mentioned proof is not suitable as a proof, and 'external' here means reality. His statement: 'and the truth of the external negative judgment' does not intend by 'external' what is customary among them, for the judgment here is not about individuals, let alone verified ones, rather the external is the locus of the judgment. And just as the negative is true, similarly the affirmative with negative predicate (i.e., the subject is non-existent) is true, for there is no affirmation in reality, but mere consideration. So it is not objected that if the mentioned negative is true, then the affirmative with negative predicate is true because they are equivalent, but its truth is impossible because it would require establishing a negation of a thing of itself. His statement: 'due to the absence of the subject in the external' means due to the removal of the subject, i.e., the concept of humanity completely in reality, just as the truth of the customary external negative judgment occurs due to the absence of individuals of the subject in the external. His statement: 'and the truth of the external negative judgment, etc.' It was said: there is discussion in this; because the proposition 'humanity is humanity', and similarly for every quiddity, is a mental proposition, so its negative, if true due to absence of subject, would be true due to its absence in the mind, not due to its absence in the external as he claimed. In summary, the proponent of quiddities being made says that the fact that humanity is humanity in reality is by the maker's making, not that it is humanity in the external thereby. For what belongs to it then is the made-ness of identity, because man in the external is identical to identity, and there is no discussion about that. The denier of their being made says: if humanity were made, then humanity would not be humanity in reality when making is absent. Then the answer that the truth of the negative is due to the absence of the subject in the external is not appropriate. So reflect.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the proof that quiddities are not made, followed by a detailed answer. The objector suggests that the absence of making might be impossible, which would entail impossibility. The answer begins by distinguishing necessary from possible: the absence of making is not impossible in itself, otherwise making would be necessary. If making is possible, its absence is possible. Then the answer addresses the claim that negating a thing of itself is impossible: when the maker's making is absent, the quiddity is completely absent from the external world, so the negative judgment 'humanity is not humanity' is true due to lack of subject, not as a contradiction. The commentator then clarifies the meaning of 'external' in this context, distinguishing it from the customary usage. There is further discussion about the truth of negative judgments and the identity of quiddities. The passage concludes with a note that the answer may not be sufficient if the quiddity is considered in itself rather than in the external. This is a complex dialectical exchange central to the debate on whether quiddities are made.
