Are Essences Made? The Mursad's Argument
Al-Iji argues that essences are not made in themselves but are made in being existent, using the dyer analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that essences are not made in themselves but are made in being existent, and why this distinction is not contradictory.

Core Idea
Essences are not made in themselves, but are made in being existent.

Al-Iji addresses a subtle theological question: Are essences themselves created? He argues that if an essence were simply itself, no making is conceivable. However, he introduces a second sense: the agent makes the essence existent, not the essence itself. The dyer analogy clarifies this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the flow. Note the two different senses of 'making'.
This passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the negative argument
Identify why making is inconceivable for an essence in itself: there is no distinction between essence and itself.
This uses the concept of مغايرة (distinction).
3
Understand the positive argument with the dyer analogy
See how the dyer makes the garment qualified by dye without making the garment itself. Apply this to God and essences.
The point is that making affects the qualification, not the essence itself.
4
Reconcile the two statements
The passage concludes that negating and affirming made-ness are both correct in different senses. Understand why there is no contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد السادس : الماهيات هل هي مجعولة أم لا معها مفهوماً سواها، لم يعقل هناك جعل، إذ لا مغايرة بين الماهية ونفسها حتى يتصور توسط جعل جاعل بينهما، فتكون إحداهما مجعولة تلك الأخرى. وكذا لا يتصور تأثير الفاعل في الوجود بمعني جعل الوجود وجوداً ، بل تأثيره في الماهية باعتبار الوجود، بمعنى أنه يجعلها متصفة بالوجود، لا بمعنى أنه يجعل اتصافها موجوداً متحققاً في الخارج، فإن الصباغ مثلاً إذا صبغ ثوباً، فإنه لا يجعل الثوب ثوباً، ولا الصبغ صبغا، بل يجعل الثوب متصفا بالصبغ في الخارج وإن لم يجعل اتصافه به موجوداً ثابتاً في الخارج، فليست الماهيات في أنفسها مجعولةً، ولا وجوداتها أيضاً في أنفسها مجعولة، بل الماهيات في كونها موجودة مجعولة وهذا المعنى مما لا ينبغي أن ينازع فيه ولا منافاة بين نفي المجعولية عن الماهيات بالمعنى الذي ذكرناه أولاً، وبين إثباتها لها بما بينا آنفا أنه الحق الذي لا يتوهم بطلانه. فالقول بنفي المجعولية مطلقاً، وبإثباتها مطلقاً كلاهما صحيح ، إذا حملا على ما صورناه .
The Second Observatory - The Sixth Purpose: Are essences made or not? With it as a concept other than itself, no making is conceivable, since there is no distinction between the essence and itself such that the mediation of a maker's making between them can be imagined, so that one of them would be made by that other. Likewise, the agent's effect on existence in the sense of making existence existence is not conceivable, rather his effect is on the essence with respect to existence, in the sense that he makes it qualified by existence, not in the sense that he makes its qualification existent and realized in the external. For the dyer, for example, when he dyes a garment, he does not make the garment a garment, nor the dye dye, but he makes the garment qualified by the dye in the external, even if he does not make its qualification existent and established in the external. Thus essences are not made in themselves, nor are their existences in themselves made, but essences are made in being existent, and this meaning is not to be disputed, and there is no contradiction between negating made-ness from essences in the sense we first mentioned and affirming it for them in the sense we just explained, which is the truth whose falsehood cannot be imagined. So the statement negating made-ness absolutely and affirming it absolutely are both correct if they are interpreted according to what we have pictured.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is the main argument of the matn. It begins by stating the question: Are essences made or not? It then argues that if an essence is not other than itself, no making is conceivable because there is no distinction between maker and made. But then it introduces a different sense: the agent's effect is on the essence with respect to existence, making it existent. The dyer analogy illustrates this: the dyer does not make the garment a garment, but makes it qualified by dye. So essences are not made in themselves, but are made in being existent. The passage concludes that denying made-ness in one sense and affirming it in another is not contradictory; both statements are correct if properly interpreted.

Key Terms
ماهية (essence)
جعل (making)
وجود (existence)
اتصاف (qualification)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying that essences are made means they are uncreated and independent of God.
Correction: The passage clarifies that essences are not made in themselves, but they are made in being existent, meaning their existence comes from God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in what sense are essences made?
A) In their own selves
B) In their existence
C) In their qualification
D) Not at all

Key Takeaway
Essences are not made in themselves, but they are made in being existent, and this distinction resolves apparent contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your essence is not made in itself, but only your existence is from Allah, how does this affect your sense of self-reliance and independence?
?
The dyer analogy shows that Allah makes you existent without changing your essence. In what ways do you try to 'make yourself' something you are not, rather than accepting the existence He gives?
?
If your very existence is a qualification given by Allah, what does that demand from you in terms of gratitude and submission right now, in this moment?

Eighth Purpose: Condition for Judging a Quiddity Composed
Al-Jurjani states the condition for judging a quiddity to be composed of parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise condition for judging a quiddity to be composed of parts, including the requirement that the shared differentiator be internal to the quiddity.

Core Idea
A quiddity is composite only when it shares an internal differentiator and differs from another.

Al-Jurjani now establishes the precise condition under which we can say a quiddity (essence) is composed of parts. This is not about mere multiplicity but about genuine composition that yields true unity. The passage clarifies that the shared differentiator must be internal to the quiddity itself, not external.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the entire Arabic passage from the source, including the matn and the commentary. Notice how the author first states the condition and then unpacks each term.
The passage is dense; you may need to read it twice.
2
Identify the condition
Find the key sentence: 'إنما يحكم بكون الماهية مركبة... إذا علم أنها مشاركة لغيرها في ذاتي... ومخالفة'. This states that a quiddity is judged composite when it is known to share an internal differentiator and to differ.
The differentiator (ذاتي) must be internal, not external.
3
Understand 'real composition'
Note the commentary explaining 'مركبة' as a real composition that gives true unity. This distinguishes a mere aggregate from a genuine composite.
Contrast with accidental aggregation.
4
Analyze the role of the differentiator
Reflect on why the differentiator must be internal (غير خارج). The commentary says it includes the entire quiddity, not just a part. This ensures the composition is essential, not accidental.
If the differentiator were external, the composition would be accidental.
5
Consider the examples of genera and differentiae
The passage mentions parts can be genera, differentiae, or others. The key is that both the shared and distinguishing aspects are included. This applies to any essential part.
The 'others' refers to non-predicated parts like matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثامن: إنما يحكم بكون الماهية مركبة من أجزاء سواء كانت أجناساً أو فصولاً أو غيرهما، إذا علم أنها مشاركة لغيرها في ذاتي أي أمر غير خارج عنها، ومخالفة. قوله : ( مركبة) أي تركيباً حقيقياً يكون بسببه المركب موصوفاً بالوحدة الحقيقية. قوله : ( أو غيرهما) أي الأجزاء الغير المحمولة. قوله : ( إذا علم إلخ) وفيه إشارة إلى أن تركب الماهية من أمرين متساويين في الصدق وفي التحقيق مجرد احتمال عقلي لا طريق لنا إلى العلم به. قوله : ( أمر) أي سواء كان محمولاً أو غير محمول. قوله : ( غير خارج) لم يفسر الذاتي بالأمر الداخل لأنه لا يحتاج في العلم بتركب الماهية. قوله : ( لكن) يكون قيام بعضها به شرطاً الخ لا يخفى أن مجرد الشرطية لا يكفي في الوحدة الحقيقية فاعتبر اللون المشروط بالضوء على أن توقف الوحدة الحقيقية على ذلك ممنوع؛ لجواز الارتباط بين الأجزاء بوجه آخر. قوله : ( سواء كانت أجناساً أو فصولاً أو غيرها) أي سواء كان بعض تلك الأجزاء أجناساً وبعضها فصولاً أو غيرها بأن يكون ما به الاشتراك فصلاً بعيداً، وما به الامتياز فصلاً قريباً مثلاً، فإن المقصود هاهنا لزوم دخول ما به الاشتراك وما به الامتياز ليس إلا. وحمل الغير على الأجزاء الخارجية، أو التعين يأباه السياق. قوله : ( أي أمر غير خارج) إنما فسر الذاتي بهذا ليشمل تمام الماهية، إذ لو أريد به الجزء لقال: داخل.
The eighth purpose: One only judges a quiddity to be composed of parts, whether they be genera or differentiae or others, when it is known that it shares with another in a differentiator (dhati) — i.e., something not external to it — and differs (from it). His statement: 'composed' i.e., a real composition by which the composite is described as having true unity. His statement: 'or others' i.e., the parts that are not predicated. His statement: 'when it is known etc.' In it there is an indication that the composition of a quiddity from two things that are equal in truth and in reality is a mere rational possibility with no way for us to know it. His statement: 'thing' i.e., whether predicated or not. His statement: 'not external' — he did not interpret 'dhati' as the internal thing because there is no need in knowing the composition of the quiddity. His statement: 'but' — the subsistence of some of them in it is a condition etc. It is not hidden that mere conditionality is insufficient for true unity, so consider color conditioned by light, for the dependence of true unity on that is denied, because connection between parts can be through another mode. His statement: 'whether they be genera or differentiae or others' i.e., whether some of those parts are genera and some differentiae or others, such that what is shared is a distant differentia and what distinguishes is a near differentia, for example, for the purpose here is nothing but the necessity of including what is shared and what distinguishes. And interpreting 'others' as external parts or determination is precluded by the context. His statement: 'i.e., something not external' — he only interpreted 'dhati' in this way to include the entire quiddity, for if he intended the part, he would have said 'internal'.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the eighth purpose: the condition for judging a quiddity to be composed. Al-Jurjani says that one only judges a quiddity to be composed of parts (whether genera, differentiae, or other) if it is known that it shares with another in a differentiator (dhati) that is not external to it, and that it differs from that other. The commentary explains that 'composed' means real composition leading to true unity. 'Others' refers to non-predicated parts. The commentary also notes that if a quiddity could be composed of two equal things, that is only a rational possibility, not knowable. The interpretation of 'dhati' as 'not external' is to include the entire quiddity, not just a part. The commentary further argues that mere conditionality (like subsistence as a condition) is insufficient for true unity; there must be a real connection, possibly through another mode. The passage also clarifies that the parts can be various combinations of genera and differentiae, and the essential point is the inclusion of both the shared and distinguishing aspects.

Key Terms
الماهية
مركبة
أجزاء
ذاتي
مشاركة
مخالفة
الوحدة الحقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any shared and distinguishing features suffice for composition, even if the differentiator is external to the quiddity.
Correction: Al-Jurjani emphasizes that the differentiator must be internal (غير خارج) to the quiddity; otherwise, the composition is not essential. The differentiator must be part of the quiddity's essence.

Checkpoint
According to the eighth purpose, when is a quiddity judged to be composed?
A) When it has parts that are independent.
B) When it is known to share a differentiator and differ from another.
C) When it is simple.
D) When it has genera only.

Key Takeaway
A quiddity is judged composite only when it shares an internal differentiator and differs from another, ensuring essential composition leading to true unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does al-Jurjani's condition challenge you to be more honest about the foundations of that unity?
?
Al-Jurjani warns that composition from two equal things is a mere rational possibility that we cannot know. Consider a belief or conviction you hold that seems perfectly logically coherent—how do you know it is genuinely composed in reality and not just a rational possibility? What would it take for you to seek certainty beyond reason?

Two Modes of Composition for Accidents
Explains two ways an accidental composite can relate to a substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways an accidental composite can inhere in a substance, and how true unity differs from mental composition.

Core Idea
Accidental composites achieve true unity only through a specific mode of inherence in a single substrate.

This passage from Sharḥ al-Mawāqif addresses a nuanced question in ontology: how can a composite accident—like a color consisting of black and white—exist as a single entity? The author explains two logical possibilities for how its parts relate to the substance they qualify. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the Kalam view of real vs. mental unity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modes
Read the passage carefully and identify the two alternatives: either all parts inhere directly with conditionality, or one part inheres directly and the other through it.
2
Understand true unity condition
Note that true unity requires the first mode with a condition that the inherence of some parts is a condition for others.
This is stated in the phrase 'حتى يتصور كون ذلك المركب واحداً حقيقياً'.
3
Analyze the example
The author gives the example of piebaldness (black and white) without such a condition, resulting only in mental composition.
The term 'بلقة' is used for piebaldness.
4
Contrast with mental composition
Reflect on how the second mode (inherence through mediation) does not produce a truly one accident but a mental unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني ) أي المركب الذي هو صفة ( يقوم بثالث ) هو غير المركب وأجزائه، فإما أن يقوم أجزاؤه كلها بذلك الثالث ابتداءً، لكن يكون قيام بعضها به شرطاً لقيام البعض الآخر، حتى يتصور كون ذلك المركب واحداً حقيقياً لا اعتبارياً . ( أو يقوم جزء منه بذلك الثالث ( ابتداء ، ( ويقوم الجزء الآخر منه بالجزء القائم به فيكون قيامه ) أي قيام الجزء الآخر ( بالثالث بالواسطة ) هي التي الجزء القائم به ابتداء . قوله : ( يقوم بثالث) لامتناع قيامه بجزئيه . قوله : ( فإما أن يقوم أجزاؤه إلخ) أي على تقدير امتناع قيام العرض بالعرض. قوله : ( حتى يتصور إلخ) وأما البلقة المركبة من السواد والبياض مع عدم اشتراط قيام أحدهما بمحله، فتركيبه اعتباري وفي الخارج بينهما التجاور .
And the second [type of composite] – i.e., the composite which is an accident – stands by a third [substance] which is other than the composite and its parts. Either all its parts stand by that third directly, but the standing of some of them to it is a condition for the standing of the others, so that that composite can be conceived as truly one, not mental (iʿtibārī). Or one part of it stands by that third directly, and the other part stands by the part that stands [to the third], so its standing – i.e., the standing of the other part – to the third is by mediation, which is the part that stands directly. His statement: 'stands by a third' because it is impossible for it to stand by its two parts. His statement: 'Either all its parts stand etc.' i.e., on the assumption of the impossibility of an accident inhering in an accident. His statement: 'so that it can be conceived etc.' As for piebaldness composed of blackness and whiteness without the condition of one's inherence in a substrate, its composition is mental, and in external reality there is adjacency between them.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage discusses the ontological composition of an accident (ʿaraḍ) that inheres in a substance. It presents two ways such a composite can exist: either all parts inhere directly in a third entity (the substrate), with the inherence of some parts being a condition for others, resulting in true unity; or one part inheres directly, and the other inheres through that first part. The commentary explains why the composite cannot inhere in its own parts, and gives an example: piebaldness (black and white) without one inhering in the substrate of the other yields only a mental composition, not real unity.

Key Terms
مركب
قيام
ثالث
عرض
اعتباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All composite accidents are real unities in the external world.
Correction: Only those accidents whose parts inhere in a substrate with a condition that the inherence of some is a condition for others are truly one; otherwise, they are only mentally composed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a composite accident considered truly one (wāḥid ḥaqīqī)?
A) When its parts inhere in each other
B) When all parts inhere directly in a third substrate but the inherence of some is a condition for others
C) When one part inheres directly and the other through that part
D) When the parts are adjacent

Key Takeaway
True unity of an accidental composite requires that all its parts inhere directly in a single substrate with a condition relationship; otherwise, the composition is only mental.

Are you willing to test whether its components really inhere in your heart with the necessary conditionality, or are they merely adjacent? What would change if you found they were only mentally unified?

The Criterion for Real Composition
Defines the condition of essential participation and differentiation for real composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the exact condition under which a quiddity is judged to be composite, and why other combinations of shared attributes do not indicate composition.

Core Idea
Real composition requires sharing an essential attribute and differing in another essential attribute.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents the precise logical criterion for recognizing true composition in quiddities. The author uses the restrictive particle 'innamā' to emphasize that only one specific pattern of shared and differing attributes counts. We'll unpack the four possible combinations and see why only one indicates genuine composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the use of 'innamā' to restrict the condition.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify the terms 'dhatī' (essential) and 'ʿaraḍ' (accidental) and their role in the argument.
3
Explore the four combinations
The author lists four possibilities for sharing and differing attributes. Trace each one and see which indicates composition.
4
Grasp the reasoning
Understand why the first combination (share in essential, differ in essential) is the only one that indicates composition, while others do not.
5
Apply the rule
Test your understanding by considering hypothetical quiddities and determining if they are composite based on this criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثامن : إنما يحكم بكون الماهية مركبة من أجزاء سواء كانت أجناساً أو فصولاً أو غيرهما ، إذا علم أنها مشاركة لغيرها في ذاتي ، أي أمر غير خارج عنها ، ومخالفة له في ذاتي آخر، لا بأن يشتركا في ذاتي ويختلفا بعارض ثبوتي أو سلب؛ لجواز كونه تمام ماهيتهما. قوله : (لا) بأن يشتركا إلخ بيان للجزء السلبي للقصر الذي يدل عليه إنما، وحاصله : أن الاشتراك في ذاتي بالمعنى المذكور فقط، أو المخالفة فيه، أو الاشتراك في العرضي فقط، أو الاختلاف فيه، لا يدل على التركيب والبساطة أصلاً، وهو ظاهر، فبقي احتمالات أحدها: الاشتراك في ذاتي والمخالفة في آخر وهذا يدل على التركيب وثانيها : الاشتراك في ذاتي والمخالفة في عرضي وثالثها الاشتراك في عرضي والاختلاف في ذاتي، ورابعها الاشتراك في عرضي، والاختلاف في عرضي آخر وشيء منها لا يدل على التركيب. والمصنف ترك الرابع لظهوره.
The Eighth Topic: A quiddity is only judged to be composed of parts – whether they be genera or specific differences or other – when it is known that it shares with another [quiddity] in an essential attribute (dhatī), i.e., a matter not external to it, and differs from it in another essential attribute; not by sharing in an essential attribute and differing by an accidental attribute (ʿaraḍ) positive or privative, because it is possible that [that essential attribute] be the complete essence of both. His statement: 'Not by sharing in an essential attribute etc.' is an explanation of the negative part of the restriction indicated by 'only' (innamā). Its summary: that sharing in an essential attribute in the mentioned sense alone, or differing in it, or sharing in an accidental attribute alone, or differing in it, does not indicate composition or simplicity at all – this is obvious. So possibilities remain: one: sharing in an essential attribute and differing in another – this indicates composition. Second: sharing in an essential attribute and differing in an accidental attribute. Third: sharing in an accidental attribute and differing in an essential attribute. Fourth: sharing in an accidental attribute and differing in another accidental attribute. None of them indicates composition. The author omitted the fourth because it is obvious.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage defines the condition under which a quiddity (māhiyya) is judged to be truly composite. It states that composition is known only when the quiddity shares an essential attribute (dhātī) with another and differs in another essential attribute. It then negates other possibilities: sharing only in an essential attribute, differing only in an essential attribute, sharing in an accidental attribute (ʿaraḍ), differing in an accidental attribute – none of these indicate composition. The reason is that if they share in an essential attribute but differ by an accident, that essential attribute might be the complete essence of both (i.e., they might be simple). The commentary then enumerates four possible combinations, showing only the first (sharing in one essential and differing in another essential) indicates composition. The author omitted the fourth combination (sharing in one accident and differing in another) because it is obviously not indicative. This passage uses the restriction particle innamā to emphasize the necessary condition.

Key Terms
ماهية
ذاتي
عارض
تركيب
بساطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two quiddities share any attribute (essential or accidental) and differ in any other attribute, they are composite.
Correction: True composition requires sharing in an essential attribute and differing in another essential attribute. Sharing in accidents or differing by accidents does not indicate composition, because the shared essential might be the complete essence.

Checkpoint
Which combination of shared and differing attributes indicates that a quiddity is truly composite?
A) Share in essential, differ in accidental
B) Share in accidental, differ in essential
C) Share in essential, differ in essential
D) Share in accidental, differ in accidental

Key Takeaway
A quiddity is only composite if it shares an essential attribute with another and differs in another essential attribute; all other patterns of sharing and differing are insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a concept you previously considered simple. After reading this lesson, can you identify if it might actually be composite based on sharing an essential with another concept and differing in another? How does this criterion change your understanding?
?
This lesson teaches that not every shared attribute indicates composition. In your own life, where have you mistakenly assumed that sharing a trait (like a habit or opinion) means you are essentially similar to someone else? How does the distinction between essential and accidental attributes clarify this?
?
When studying the divine attributes, you might be tempted to treat them as composite parts of Allah's essence. How does the criterion in this lesson warn against that error? What would it mean if Allah's attributes shared an essential with creation? Take a moment to commit to using this logical tool in your future reflections.

The Necessity of Parts Needing Each Other and the Objection from Army and Confection
Al-Jurjani states the rule, gives an example, and presents the objection with the initial answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Jurjani's rule about the necessity of parts needing each other for true composition, the objection from the army and confection, and the initial answer involving the formal part.

Core Idea
True composition requires interdependence of parts; apparent counterexamples reveal a formal part that depends on the material.

This passage establishes a key principle of composition: for parts to form a true unity, they must need each other. Al-Jurjani illustrates with a stone and a human, then addresses objections from the army and confection. The response introduces the distinction between formal and material parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the translation. Notice the flow from rule to example to objection to answer.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the rule and example
Find the statement of the rule: if parts are independent, no single essence results. Then see the example of a stone and a human.
The example is meant for clarification, not as proof.
3
Understand the objection
The army and confection seem to refute the rule because each component is independent yet they form composites.
4
See how the answer uses formal and material parts
The answer says the formal part (social form) needs the material part (the units/ingredients), so the rule holds.
This introduces key terms: juzʾ ṣūrī and juzʾ māddī.
5
Reflect on the universal judgment
Consider why the rule is self-evident and how the example and answer clarify it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : لا بد من حاجة الأجزاء بعضها إلى بعض استغنى كل من الأجزاء ( عن الآخر، لم يحصل منهما ماهية واحدة) وحدة حقيقية، كالحجر الموضوع بجنب الإنسانية قالوا هذا الحكم الكلي بديهي والتمثيل للتوضيح . وأورد العسكر فإنه مركب من الآحاد)، مع استغناء كل منها الآخر، (والمعجون ) فإنه مركب من المفردات) مع أن كل مفرد منها مستغن عما عداه فانتقض ذلك الحكم الكلي . ( وأجيب ) عنه ( بأن الجزء الصوري فيهما )، وهو الهيئة الاجتماعية العارضة للآحاد كلها وللمفردات بأسرها، ( محتاج إلى ) الجزء المادي
The second observatory - the ninth objective: There must be a need of the parts for each other. If each of the parts (is independent from the other, a single essence does not result from them) a true unity, like the stone placed beside the human. They said: This universal judgment is self-evident and the example is for clarification. And they objected with the army, for it is composed of units, while each of them is independent from the other, and the confection, for it is composed of simples, while each simple is independent from what is other than it, so that universal judgment is refuted. (And it was answered) about it (that the formal part in them), which is the social form accident to all the units and to all the simples, (needs) the material part.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of a rule, an objection, and an initial response. It begins by stating the condition for a true composite: the parts must need each other; otherwise no single essence results. The example of a stone and a human illustrates two independent things that do not form a true unity. The author then notes that this universal judgment is self-evident and the example is only for clarification. The objection is raised using the army and the confection. The army is composed of individual soldiers each independent, yet it is considered a composite. Similarly, the confection is made of simple ingredients each independent. These seem to refute the universal judgment. The answer given is that in both cases there is a formal part (juzʾ ṣūrī), namely the social form (hayʾa ijtimāʿiyya) that is accidental to the units or ingredients. This formal part needs the material part (juzʾ māddī), which is the collection of units or ingredients. Thus the requirement of mutual need is satisfied by the formal part's dependence on the material part.

Key Terms
جزء صوري
جزء مادي
هيئة اجتماعية
حكم كلي
ماهية حقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The army and confection are counterexamples because each part is independent.
Correction: They do have a formal part (social form) that depends on the material parts, so the rule holds.

Checkpoint
What is the formal part (al-juzʾ al-ṣūrī) in the army according to the initial answer?
A) The individual soldiers
B) The social form (al-hayʾa al-ijtimāʿiyya)
C) The commander
D) The military discipline

Key Takeaway
The rule that parts must need each other for true composition stands even against apparent counterexamples, because those have a formal part that depends on the material part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you learned that true unity requires parts that need each other. Consider your own life: are your various roles, habits, and goals integrated into a single coherent self that depends on each part, or are they merely stacked like a stone beside a human? Which 'formal part' gives shape to your existence, and does it truly depend on the material parts of your daily actions? What would it mean for you to ensure that everything you do contributes to one unified purpose, and how can you begin that integration today?
?
The objection from the army shows that even when parts seem independent, there can be a hidden formal part that depends on the material. Think of your prayers or acts of worship: do you sometimes perform them as independent 'units' without the 'social form' of sincerity and presence of heart that ties them together? If the formal part of your worship—your intention and awareness—does not depend on the physical acts, are those acts truly part of a single essence of devotion? How can you cultivate that formal part so that it genuinely needs the material parts of your practice?
?
The answer in the passage focuses on the formal part needing the material, not the other way around. In your relationship with Allah, is it possible that you sometimes think Allah needs your worship? This lesson reminds you that the need is only from the creation towards the Creator. How does this reality shape your understanding of your own dependency, and what concrete step can you take today to live more consciously in that state of need?

Critique of the First Answer: The Weakness of Social Form
Al-Jurjani critiques the first answer that appeals to social form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first answer (social form) is weak in explaining the composition of a confection.

Core Idea
Social form is accidental; it cannot constitute true unity.

In this passage, al-Jurjani critiques an initial attempt to explain how the simples combine into a composite. He argues that appealing to a mere social form (hayʾa ijtimāʿiyya) is insufficient because such an accidental attribute does not create a genuine essential unity. A human and a stone can share a similar social form, yet they do not form a true composite. Consequently, this answer fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. It begins mid-sentence, critiquing the 'material part' and the social form. Note the parenthetical markings which may indicate the matn.
The passage is a direct continuation from the previous page.
2
Identify the critique
The author calls the first answer 'weak' (ḍaʿīf) and explains why: the social form is accidental (ʿāriḍa). He gives an example of a human and a stone to show that such a form does not produce a true composite.
3
Understand the conclusion
If the social form were sufficient, the composite of human and stone would be a true essence, which is false by necessity. This shows the insufficiency of the first answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( المادي)، الذي هو الآحاد والمفردات، وهو ضعيف؛ لأن مثل هذه الهيئة الاعتبارية عارضة للإنسان والحجر الموضوع بجنبه، فلو كان احتياجها كافياً لكان المركب منهما ماهية حقيقية، وهو باطل بالضرورة. ( والأولى) في الجواب ( أن يقال : أما المعجون فلا بد فيه من (مزاج) أي صورة نوعية تابعة للمزاج ( يستعقب كيفيات ) وآثاراً صادرة عنه ( وأنه ( أي ذلك المزاج بمعنى الصورة جزء من المعجون، و ( محتاج إلى الأجزاء الآخر لحلوله فيها، ويؤيد ما ذكرناه قول الإمام الرازي في المباحث المشرقية وأما الجزء الآخر، وهو الصورة المعجونية التي مبدأ الآثار الصادرة عنه، فهي محتاجة إلى الجزء الأول الذي هو مجموع المفردات، وعلى هذا فلا إشكال.
(The material part), which is the units and simples, and it is weak; because such an accidental form is accident to the human and the stone placed beside him; if its need were sufficient, the composite of the two would be a true essence, which is necessarily false. (And the better) in the answer (is to say: As for the confection, there must be in it a (mixture) i.e., a specific form consequent to the mixture (which entails qualities) and effects emanating from it (and that it) (i.e., that mixture in the sense of the form is a part of the confection, and (needs the other parts for its immanence in them, and what we have said is supported by the statement of Imam al-Razi in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya: As for the other part, which is the confectional form that is the principle of the effects emanating from it, it is in need of the first part which is the aggregate of the simples, and on this there is no difficulty.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage first critiques the initial answer. It points out that the social form (hayʾa ijtimāʿiyya) is merely an accidental attribute, not a true part that creates unity. For example, if we consider a human and a stone placed beside him, the same social form could be attributed to them, but that does not make them a true composite. Therefore, the initial answer is weak. Then it presents the preferred answer. For the confection (ma'jūn), there must be a mizāj (mixture) which is a specific form (ṣūra nawʿiyya) that results from the mixture and is the source of its properties. This mizāj, understood as a form, is a part of the confection and depends on the other parts (the simples) for its existence in them. The text supports this with a quote from Imam al-Razi, who distinguishes between the material aggregate and the confectional form that needs it. Thus, the true composition is achieved through this substantial form that really depends on the material parts, unlike the accidental social form.

عارضة
ماهية حقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any relational attribute can unify parts into a composite.
Correction: The social form is accidental and does not create essential unity; only a substantial form can do that.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Jurjani reject the first answer?
A) Because the social form is eternal
B) Because the social form is accidental and does not create true unity
C) Because the simples are sufficient
D) Because the human and stone example proves the opposite

Key Takeaway
The social form is insufficient because it is accidental and does not constitute a true essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your relationships (with family, friends, or your community), do you rely on superficial social forms—shared routines, appearances, or rituals—rather than a deep, essential bond? ?
Al-Jurjani warns against settling for a weak, insufficient answer. In your own faith, is there any belief or practice you hold merely because it is socially convenient or traditional, without it being a core, essential truth for you? What is it, and will you commit to either making it essential or letting it go?

The Preferred Answer: Mizaj as a Specific Form
Al-Jurjani presents the preferred answer using mizaj as a specific form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the preferred answer that a confection's composition is due to a mizaj (mixture) as a specific form (ṣūra nawʿiyya) that is a part of the composite and depends on the simples.

Core Idea
Mizaj as a specific form provides the true substantial unity.

After dismissing the first answer, al-Jurjani presents the better explanation. The confection (ma'jūn) must have a mixture (mizāj) that gives rise to qualities and effects. This mizaj is a specific form (ṣūra nawʿiyya) that is part of the composite and depends on the simpler parts for its immanence. He supports this with a quote from Imam al-Razi, who distinguishes between the aggregate of simples and the confectional form that needs them. This resolves the difficulty.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text for the preferred answer. Note the citation of Imam al-Razi and the terminology of mizaj and ṣūra nawʿiyya.
The parenthetical phrases may indicate the matn being commented upon.
2
Understand the role of mizaj
Al-Jurjani states that the confection requires a mizaj, which he interprets as a specific form (ṣūra nawʿiyya). This form is consequent upon the mixture and is the source of the confection's qualities and effects.
3
Analyze the part-whole relationship
The mizaj (as form) is a part of the confection and needs the other parts (the simples) to inhere in them. This explains how the confection is a true composite.
4
Review al-Razi's support
Imam al-Razi confirms that the confectional form (al-ṣūra al-maʿjūniyya) is the principle of effects and needs the aggregate of simples. This corroborates the preferred answer.

Impossibility of Accident as Part of Substance
Sanusi argues why an accident cannot be a part of a substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an accident cannot be a part of a substance and the logical contradiction that would arise.

Core Idea
An accident cannot be a part of a substance without causing a contradiction in mode of existence.

Sanusi begins by asserting that parts necessarily need each other, then demonstrates that an accident cannot be a part of a substance. The argument rests on the impossibility of one thing being both substance and accident in the same mode of existence. This foundational principle underlies the rest of the discussion on composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening proposition
Start with the statement that parts need each other. Note the condition: something posterior cannot be a part unless composed of another substance and an accident.
Pay attention to the causal language: 'because then one of the two parts would be posterior to the other.'
2
Identify the impossibility claim
Find the sentence 'it is impossible for an accident to be a part predicated of a substance'. This is the core ruling being defended.
3
Understand the reasoning
The commentary explains that this impossibility is because it would require a single thing to be both substance and accident in one mode of existence, which is impermissible.
Consider the distinction between one mode and two modes.
4
Reflect on the bed example
The commentary uses a bed as an example: if the arrangement (form) is considered a part, the bed would not exist externally, showing that such a part cannot be an accident.
The truth is that the bed is the wooden pieces subject to arrangement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : لا بد من حاجة الأجزاء بعضها إلى بعض فإنه متأخر عنه، فلا يكون جزءاً منه دون تركيبه من جوهر آخر وعرض يقوم بذلك آحاد الجوهر الآخر؛ لأن اللازم حينئذ تأخر أحد الجزئين عن الآخر، نعم يستحيل أن يكون العرض جزءاً محمولاً للجوهر فتأمل . ( وأما العسكر فإنه ) عبارة عن مجموع فقط، وهو موجود بلا شبهة إلا أنه ( ماهية ) وحدتها ( اعتبارية، والكلام في الماهية الحقيقية الواحدة، ولا فرق بين العسكر والمركب من الإنسان والحجر في أن المركب فيهما عين الآحاد بأسرها، وفي أنه يترتب على الكل فيهما ما لا يترتب على كل واحد من أجزائه، وفي أنه يمكن أن يعتبر هناك هيئة اجتماعية باعتبارها تعرض للأمور المتعددة وحدة اعتبارية إلا أن تلك الهيئة إذا اعتبرت وجعلت جزءاً من العسكر مثلاً، لم يكن العسكر أمراً موجوداً في الخارج؛ لأن ما جزؤه عدم فهو عدم. قوله : ( وقال ) أي ذلك البعض . قوله : (يستحيل إلخ) بناء على أنه يلزم أن يكون شيء واحد جوهراً وعرضاً في نحو واحد من الوجود، وذا لا يجوز إنما الجائز جوازه في نحوين منه . قوله: (فتأمل وجهه أن ذلك إنما يتم إذا كان الترتيب أو الهيئة المترتبة موجوداً في الخارج، وأما إذا كان اعتبارياً، فجزئيته تستلزم عدم السرير في الخارج فالحق أنه عبارة عن القطع الخشبية المعروضة للترتيب أو الهيئة . قوله : (إلا) أن تلك الهيئة إلخ) لا فرق بينهما إلا بأنه في أن آحادهما موجودة، فيكون الكل موجوداً. وبعد اعتبار الهيئة الاجتماعية يكون المركب اعتبارياً موصوفاً بالوحدة الاعتبارية معدوماً في الخارج، إلا أن القول بعدم وجود العسكر في الخارج، مما لا يقول به عاقل، بخلاف الحجر الموضوع بجنب الإنسان. ومن هذا علم أنه على تقدير التركيب لا بد من الهيئة الاجتماعية، سواء كان المركب حقيقياً أو اعتبارياً، فهذا لا ينافي ما ذكره الشارح قدس سره في حواشي المطالع من أن كل مركب لا بد فيه من هيئة اجتماعية وحدانية تكون جزءاً من المركب. والمراد بالهيئة الاجتماعية الجزء الصوري؛ ليطرد في الجسم المركب من الهيولى والصورة، على ما فسره في تلك الحواشي في بحث تقسيم العلم وفي مباحث التعريفات، فلا يرد النقض بالجسم المركب من الهيولى والصورة وأنه يلزم أن يكون كل مركب جوهري متقوماً بالعرض. قوله : ( والكلام في الماهية الحقيقية (الواحدة فإن قلت : كل ماهية لها وحدة ولو بحسب الهيئة الاعتبارية، يحتاج جزؤه الصوري، أعني تلك الهيئة إلى باقي الأجزاء، فما معنى تخصيص ما له وحدة حقيقية بهذا الحكم؟ قلت مرادهم احتياج معروض الهيئة، فإن الهيئة ثابتة في الحقيقيات، وإن لم تكن جزءاً، وأجزاء المعدن هي العناصر الممتزجة، فمن حيث الامتزاج يشترط كل منها بالآخر، فلا يبعد اعتبار الأجزاء المادية في الحاجة، ولك أن تقول : المراد الحاجة بحسب نفس الأمر، وحاجة الهيئة الاعتبارية محضة.
The Second Observation - Ninth Section: The parts necessarily need each other. For if something is posterior to another, it cannot be a part of it without being composed of another substance and an accident that subsists in that other substance; because then one of the two parts would be posterior to the other. Indeed, it is impossible for an accident to be a part predicated of a substance, so reflect. (As for the army) it is merely a collection, and it exists without doubt, except that it is a quiddity whose unity is accidental. The discussion is about the single real quiddity. There is no difference between the army and the composite of a man and a stone in that the composite in both is exactly the individuals entirely, and in that consequences apply to the whole in both that do not apply to each individual part, and in that a social form can be considered by which a certain accidental unity attaches to the multiple things. However, if that form is considered and made a part of the army, for example, then the army would not be something existing externally; because what has a part that is non-existent is itself non-existent. His statement: (He said) i.e., that part. His statement: (It is impossible, etc.) based on the fact that it would necessitate that one thing be both substance and accident in one mode of existence, and that is not permissible; rather, what is permissible is that it be both in two modes. His statement: (Reflect) meaning that this only holds if the arrangement or the resulting form exists externally. But if it is accidental, then its being a part entails the non-existence of the bed externally; the truth is that it consists of the wooden pieces that are subject to arrangement or form. His statement: (Except that that form, etc.) There is no difference between them except that in one the individuals exist, so the whole exists. After considering the social form, the composite becomes accidental, described with accidental unity, non-existent externally; however, the statement that the army does not exist externally is something no rational person would say, unlike a stone placed next to a person. From this it is known that given composition, a social form is necessary, whether the composite is real or accidental. This does not contradict what the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) mentioned in his glosses on al-Mutali' that every composite must have a unifying social form that is a part of the composite. By 'social form' is meant the formal part; this applies consistently to the body composed of matter and form, as he explained in those glosses in the section on the division of knowledge and in the discussions of definitions. Thus the objection is not upheld concerning the body composed of matter and form, that it would necessitate that every substantial composite be constituted by an accident. His statement: (And the discussion is about the real quiddity) For if you say: Every quiddity has unity, even if only according to the accidental form; its formal part, i.e., that form, needs the remaining parts. So what is the meaning of specifying what has real unity with this ruling? I say: They mean the need of the substrate of the form. For the form is established in real things even if it is not a part. The parts of a mineral are the mixed elements; from the perspective of mixture, each of them requires the other. So it is not unlikely to consider the material parts in need. And you may say: What is meant is need in itself, and the need of the accidental form is merely mental.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument and commentary on the necessity of parts needing each other in a real composite. The author begins by stating that parts necessarily need each other, and then explains that if something is posterior to another, it cannot be its part unless it is composed of another substance and an accident. However, an accident cannot be a part of a substance because that would make one thing both substance and accident. The commentary clarifies that this impossibility applies when considering one mode of existence; in two modes it is possible.

The discussion then turns to the example of an army (al-'askar) as an accidental composite. The army is a collection whose unity is merely accidental (i'tibariyya), not real. The author distinguishes between real composites (like a substance) and accidental composites (like an army). Both have individuals existing, but in an accidental composite, the whole is just the individuals, whereas in a real composite, the whole has a real unity. The commentary explains that if a social form (hay'a ijtimaiyya) is considered a part, the composite would not exist externally because its part would be non-existent. However, no rational person denies the existence of an army externally, unlike a random collection. Thus, a social form is necessary for any composite, real or accidental. The commentator reconciles this with earlier glosses, stating that the social form is the formal part (al-juz' al-suri), which applies to hylomorphic compounds as well. Finally, a question about why only real quiddities are specified for this need is answered: the need refers to the substrate of the form, and the form is present in real things even if not a part. The mineral example illustrates mutual need due to mixture.

Key Terms
جوهر
عرض
جزء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An accident can be a part of a substance.
Correction: The text argues that this would make one thing both substance and accident in the same mode of existence, which is impossible. An accident cannot be a part of a substance.

Checkpoint
Why cannot an accident be a part of a substance?
A) Because it would make the composite non-existent.
B) Because it would require one thing to be both substance and accident in the same mode of existence.
C) Because accidents are posterior to substances.
D) Because a substance cannot have any parts.

Key Takeaway
It is impossible for an accident to be a part of a substance because it would require a single entity to be both substance and accident in the same mode of existence.

The Army as Accidental Composite and the Social Form
Sanusi analyzes the army as an accidental composite and argues for the necessity of a social form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between real and accidental composites through the example of an army and the concept of a social form.

Core Idea
An accidental composite like an army has merely intentional unity, but still requires a social form to be considered a composite.

Sanusi uses the example of an army to illustrate an accidental composite. He argues that while an army is a collection with accidental unity, it still requires a social form (hay'a ijtimaiyya) to be considered a composite. The discussion clarifies that both real and accidental composites need such a form, and reconciles this with earlier glosses on hylomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the army example
Start with the sentence 'As for the army, it is merely a collective'. Note that it exists but its unity is accidental (i'tibariyya).
The discussion aims to contrast with real quiddity.
2
Compare army and stone-man composite
Notice the similarities: both are exactly the sum of individuals, and both have consequences for the whole not applicable to parts.
The difference lies in the nature of unity.
3
Understand the social form condition
If a social form is considered a part, the army would not exist externally because its part would be non-existent. Yet rational people affirm the army's existence.
This shows that the social form cannot be a part in the same way.
4
Learn the necessity of social form
The conclusion: given composition, a social form is necessary for both real and accidental composites, but it is not an external part; it is a formal part (al-juz' al-suri).
This reconciles with earlier glosses on matter and form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : لا بد من حاجة الأجزاء بعضها إلى بعض فإنه متأخر عنه، فلا يكون جزءاً منه دون تركيبه من جوهر آخر وعرض يقوم بذلك آحاد الجوهر الآخر؛ لأن اللازم حينئذ تأخر أحد الجزئين عن الآخر، نعم يستحيل أن يكون العرض جزءاً محمولاً للجوهر فتأمل . ( وأما العسكر فإنه ) عبارة عن مجموع فقط، وهو موجود بلا شبهة إلا أنه ( ماهية ) وحدتها ( اعتبارية، والكلام في الماهية الحقيقية الواحدة، ولا فرق بين العسكر والمركب من الإنسان والحجر في أن المركب فيهما عين الآحاد بأسرها، وفي أنه يترتب على الكل فيهما ما لا يترتب على كل واحد من أجزائه، وفي أنه يمكن أن يعتبر هناك هيئة اجتماعية باعتبارها تعرض للأمور المتعددة وحدة اعتبارية إلا أن تلك الهيئة إذا اعتبرت وجعلت جزءاً من العسكر مثلاً، لم يكن العسكر أمراً موجوداً في الخارج؛ لأن ما جزؤه عدم فهو عدم. قوله : ( وقال ) أي ذلك البعض . قوله : (يستحيل إلخ) بناء على أنه يلزم أن يكون شيء واحد جوهراً وعرضاً في نحو واحد من الوجود، وذا لا يجوز إنما الجائز جوازه في نحوين منه . قوله: (فتأمل وجهه أن ذلك إنما يتم إذا كان الترتيب أو الهيئة المترتبة موجوداً في الخارج، وأما إذا كان اعتبارياً، فجزئيته تستلزم عدم السرير في الخارج فالحق أنه عبارة عن القطع الخشبية المعروضة للترتيب أو الهيئة . قوله : (إلا) أن تلك الهيئة إلخ) لا فرق بينهما إلا بأنه في أن آحادهما موجودة، فيكون الكل موجوداً. وبعد اعتبار الهيئة الاجتماعية يكون المركب اعتبارياً موصوفاً بالوحدة الاعتبارية معدوماً في الخارج، إلا أن القول بعدم وجود العسكر في الخارج، مما لا يقول به عاقل، بخلاف الحجر الموضوع بجنب الإنسان. ومن هذا علم أنه على تقدير التركيب لا بد من الهيئة الاجتماعية، سواء كان المركب حقيقياً أو اعتبارياً، فهذا لا ينافي ما ذكره الشارح قدس سره في حواشي المطالع من أن كل مركب لا بد فيه من هيئة اجتماعية وحدانية تكون جزءاً من المركب. والمراد بالهيئة الاجتماعية الجزء الصوري؛ ليطرد في الجسم المركب من الهيولى والصورة، على ما فسره في تلك الحواشي في بحث تقسيم العلم وفي مباحث التعريفات، فلا يرد النقض بالجسم المركب من الهيولى والصورة وأنه يلزم أن يكون كل مركب جوهري متقوماً بالعرض. قوله : ( والكلام في الماهية الحقيقية (الواحدة فإن قلت : كل ماهية لها وحدة ولو بحسب الهيئة الاعتبارية، يحتاج جزؤه الصوري، أعني تلك الهيئة إلى باقي الأجزاء، فما معنى تخصيص ما له وحدة حقيقية بهذا الحكم؟ قلت مرادهم احتياج معروض الهيئة، فإن الهيئة ثابتة في الحقيقيات، وإن لم تكن جزءاً، وأجزاء المعدن هي العناصر الممتزجة، فمن حيث الامتزاج يشترط كل منها بالآخر، فلا يبعد اعتبار الأجزاء المادية في الحاجة، ولك أن تقول : المراد الحاجة بحسب نفس الأمر، وحاجة الهيئة الاعتبارية محضة.
The Second Observation - Ninth Section: The parts necessarily need each other. For if something is posterior to another, it cannot be a part of it without being composed of another substance and an accident that subsists in that other substance; because then one of the two parts would be posterior to the other. Indeed, it is impossible for an accident to be a part predicated of a substance, so reflect. (As for the army) it is merely a collection, and it exists without doubt, except that it is a quiddity whose unity is accidental. The discussion is about the single real quiddity. There is no difference between the army and the composite of a man and a stone in that the composite in both is exactly the individuals entirely, and in that consequences apply to the whole in both that do not apply to each individual part, and in that a social form can be considered by which a certain accidental unity attaches to the multiple things. However, if that form is considered and made a part of the army, for example, then the army would not be something existing externally; because what has a part that is non-existent is itself non-existent. His statement: (He said) i.e., that part. His statement: (It is impossible, etc.) based on the fact that it would necessitate that one thing be both substance and accident in one mode of existence, and that is not permissible; rather, what is permissible is that it be both in two modes. His statement: (Reflect) meaning that this only holds if the arrangement or the resulting form exists externally. But if it is accidental, then its being a part entails the non-existence of the bed externally; the truth is that it consists of the wooden pieces that are subject to arrangement or form. His statement: (Except that that form, etc.) There is no difference between them except that in one the individuals exist, so the whole exists. After considering the social form, the composite becomes accidental, described with accidental unity, non-existent externally; however, the statement that the army does not exist externally is something no rational person would say, unlike a stone placed next to a person. From this it is known that given composition, a social form is necessary, whether the composite is real or accidental. This does not contradict what the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) mentioned in his glosses on al-Mutali' that every composite must have a unifying social form that is a part of the composite. By 'social form' is meant the formal part; this applies consistently to the body composed of matter and form, as he explained in those glosses in the section on the division of knowledge and in the discussions of definitions. Thus the objection is not upheld concerning the body composed of matter and form, that it would necessitate that every substantial composite be constituted by an accident. His statement: (And the discussion is about the real quiddity) For if you say: Every quiddity has unity, even if only according to the accidental form; its formal part, i.e., that form, needs the remaining parts. So what is the meaning of specifying what has real unity with this ruling? I say: They mean the need of the substrate of the form. For the form is established in real things even if it is not a part. The parts of a mineral are the mixed elements; from the perspective of mixture, each of them requires the other. So it is not unlikely to consider the material parts in need. And you may say: What is meant is need in itself, and the need of the accidental form is merely mental.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument and commentary on the necessity of parts needing each other in a real composite. The author begins by stating that parts necessarily need each other, and then explains that if something is posterior to another, it cannot be its part unless it is composed of another substance and an accident. However, an accident cannot be a part of a substance because that would make one thing both substance and accident. The commentary clarifies that this impossibility applies when considering one mode of existence; in two modes it is possible.

The discussion then turns to the example of an army (al-'askar) as an accidental composite. The army is a collection whose unity is merely accidental (i'tibariyya), not real. The author distinguishes between real composites (like a substance) and accidental composites (like an army). Both have individuals existing, but in an accidental composite, the whole is just the individuals, whereas in a real composite, the whole has a real unity. The commentary explains that if a social form (hay'a ijtimaiyya) is considered a part, the composite would not exist externally because its part would be non-existent. However, no rational person denies the existence of an army externally, unlike a random collection. Thus, a social form is necessary for any composite, real or accidental. The commentator reconciles this with earlier glosses, stating that the social form is the formal part (al-juz' al-suri), which applies to hylomorphic compounds as well. Finally, a question about why only real quiddities are specified for this need is answered: the need refers to the substrate of the form, and the form is present in real things even if not a part. The mineral example illustrates mutual need due to mixture.

ماهية اعتبارية
هيئة اجتماعية
جزء صوري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidental composites like an army have no real existence at all.
Correction: The text affirms the existence of an army externally; it is the unity that is accidental, not the existence itself.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the need for a social form in composites?
A) Only real composites need a social form.
B) Only accidental composites need a social form.
C) Both real and accidental composites need a social form.
D) Neither type of composite needs a social form.

Key Takeaway
Both real and accidental composites require a social form to be considered a composite, but in accidental composites the form is not a real part of the existing entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are part of many accidental composites—your family, your community, your nation. Are you treating these groups as real unities with genuine responsibilities, or as mere mental fictions you can dismiss when inconvenient? How does Sanusi's insistence on their existence (while not being real parts) demand you take your social roles seriously?
?
If the social form that binds you to others is not a real part of your essence, what keeps you from dissolving that bond whenever it becomes difficult? What does your willingness (or unwillingness) to maintain that bond reveal about your own iman and sense of accountability?
?
You mentally unify people into an 'army' under a leader or cause. But if the unity is only in your mind, is your loyalty to them any less real? When you betray that unity, are you betraying something that exists or only a figment? Let this question settle: what does the existence of accidental composites say about your duty to maintain them?

The Need in Real Quiddities vs. Accidental Forms
Sanusi explains the need of real quiddities versus accidental forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why real quiddities are specified as needing each other, and how need differs for accidental forms.

Core Idea
Real quiddities need each other in themselves, while accidental forms need only in a mental sense.

A potential objection is raised: every quiddity has a formal part that needs other parts, so why specify only real quiddities? Sanusi answers by distinguishing the need of the substrate (the form's bearer) from the need of the form itself. In real composites, the components need each other in reality; in accidental ones, the need is merely mental.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start with the objection: 'Every quiddity has unity... its formal part needs the remaining parts. Why specify only those with real unity?'
This challenges the earlier focus on real quiddities.
2
Understand the answer
The response: the need refers to the substrate of the form. In real things, the form is established even if not a part, and the material parts (like elements in a mineral) need each other.
The example of a mineral shows mutual need due to mixture.
3
Distinguish two types of need
The need of an accidental form is merely mental (i'tibariyya), while the need in real quiddities is in itself.
This clarifies the specification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a relationship or commitment in your life that you consider 'real'. Is its need for the other person merely mental—something you imagine—or does it touch the very essence of who you are? How does this lesson on real versus mental need challenge you to evaluate what you truly depend on?
?

?
The accidental form's need is only in the mind—a social role, a status. Examine yourself: are you investing your deepest need for meaning in such mental constructs? If so, where will you find a need that is real, not merely mental, to anchor your life?

The Condition of Non-Circularity in Interdependence
The author states the necessity of interdependence and the condition of non-circularity with the hyle-form example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of circularity (al-dawr) and why interdependence between parts must be from two different aspects to avoid it, using the example of matter and form.

Core Idea
Interdependence is necessary, but it must be two-way from different aspects to avoid circularity.

The author establishes that parts of a whole necessarily need each other, but this need must not be circular. Circularity occurs when each part needs the other from the same aspect. The classic example is matter and form: each requires the other from a different perspective. This lesson unpacks this condition and its importance in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice the emphatic opening that no rational person would deny the necessity of interdependence.
The passage uses technical terms like 'dawr' (circularity) and 'hayula' (matter).
2
Identify the condition of non-circularity
The text states that the need between parts must not entail circularity. Pay attention to how the author defines circularity: each part needing the other from one side.
3
Distinguish one-sided vs. two-sided need
Understand that one-sided need leads to circularity, while two-sided need from different aspects is permissible and avoids it.
The example of matter and form illustrates this two-sided need: matter needs form for subsistence, form needs matter for individuation.
4
Analyze the hyle-form example
Examine how matter and form have a mutual need from different aspects, making it valid. Consider why this does not fall into circularity.
This example is foundational and will be revisited in later sections on substances.
5
Connect to the broader argument
Reflect on why this condition is necessary for any discussion of composite quiddities. It prepares the ground for the next section on genus and species.
The author signals that this will be discussed further in the section on substances.

The Second Observation - Tenth Section: The necessity of some parts needing others, absolutely, and that is something no rational person would deny. (Then the need) between parts must be either one-sided or two-sided (in such a way that it does not entail circularity). That is, its entailing circularity (is that each part needs the other from one aspect. But) each part needing the other (from two aspects is permissible), since there is no circularity in it, (just as matter) needs form (from one aspect, which is that matter subsists through form, and) form needs matter (from) another (aspect), which is its need for matter for its individuation. (And this will come) later in the section on substances. [Tenth Section: Necessity of some parts needing others] (The philosophers said: The necessity of some parts needing others has become apparent) in a single quiddity that has real unity, and there is no doubt that the quiddity composed of genus and species is a single real quiddity as well, so there must be a need between them. (So one of them is a cause of the other) and the genus is not a cause of the species, otherwise (it would entail it and the genus would be confined to one species, or we would say: the species... His statement: (And there is no doubt, etc.) He indicated by assuming this premise that in the phrasing of the text there is ellipsis by contextual indication. This is according to the view of those who say that the predicated parts are distinct in external reality as quiddities, whether they are united in existence or not. As for the view of those who hold that they are extracted (from the mind), there is nothing externally except the simple entity, and composition from them in the mind is accidental. His statement: (a single real quiddity as well) i.e., with real unity, i.e., disregarding the consideration of the considerer. According to the view that the quiddity is composed of predicated parts externally, its attribution of unity is external; according to the view that it is extracted and composition is only in the mind, its attribution of unity is in the mind. His statement: (And the genus would be confined, etc.) because it is a cause in relation to its effect for the composition of the real quiddity from them, so its nature does not exist separate from it, since the single nature does not entail two things. His statement: (Either from one side can be included in not entailing circularity, but the more apparent is that his statement: in such a way that it does not entail circularity concerns when the need is from both sides. His statement: (And there is no doubt that the quiddity composed of genus and species is a single real quiddity as well) It is said: If one makes 'a reality' as the predicate so that the proposition is indefinite, because among composites there are accidental ones, which are not suitable, so the proper way is to make it a distinguishing word or a circumstantial, and 'one' is the predicate so that the proposition is universal, not indefinite. His statement: (So one of them is a cause of the other) By 'cause' is meant that on which a thing depends in general, so it includes the condition, and the objection to it does not stand; rather, it repels his statement: 'the genus is not a cause of the species', as will be clarified. His statement: (Or we say, etc.) The meaning of the alternative is to choose between the two expressions in necessitating corruption.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth section, which asserts the necessity of some parts needing others. It begins by affirming that necessity absolutely, and then specifies the condition: the need between parts must be such that it does not entail circularity (al-dawr). Circularity occurs when each part needs the other from one aspect. If the need is from two aspects, it is permissible and does not constitute circularity. The example given is hyle (matter) and form: matter needs form for its subsistence, and form needs matter for its individuation—a two-way dependency from different aspects. The author notes that this will be discussed later in the section on substances.

Then the philosophers' statement is introduced: the quiddity composed of genus and species is a single real quiddity, so the genus and species must have a need between them; one must be a cause of the other. However, the genus cannot be the cause of the species, because if it were, the genus would be confined to that species, and the species would not be distinct. The commentary explains the background: there are two views on the nature of predicated parts—one holds they are distinct externally, the other that composition is only in the mind. The commentator clarifies the proper reading of the text, arguing that 'one' (wahida) should be the predicate to make the proposition universal. The term 'cause' here is broad, including conditions, so it does not fall into objections. The alternative expression 'or we would say' indicates a choice between two ways of showing the absurdity of the genus causing the species.

Key Terms
دور
هيولى
صورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if parts need each other, it always leads to circularity.
Correction: The author shows that two-way need from different aspects is valid and does not entail circularity, as in the matter-form example.

Checkpoint
What is the condition for interdependence between parts to avoid circularity?
A) The need must be one-sided.
B) The need must be from two different aspects.
C) The need must be from the same aspect.
D) The need must be absolute.

Key Takeaway
Interdependence between parts is necessary, but it must be from two different aspects to avoid circularity, as illustrated by matter and form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a relationship in your life where you expect the same thing from someone that they expect from you, creating a cycle of frustration? How can you break that cycle by recognizing the different aspects of need?
?
The author says no rational person denies the need for others. But we often pretend we are self-sufficient. In what area of your life are you denying your need for others, and what would it look like to admit that need from a specific aspect?
?
Consider your relationship with Allah. Do you sometimes expect from Him what you think He should expect from you, as if on the same level? How does the distinction of aspects (e.g., our need for His mercy vs. His right to be worshipped) clarify your duty?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
7 min
The Genus-Species Causal Relation
The author argues that genus and species must have a causal relation and the genus cannot cause the species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why one of the genus and species must be a cause of the other, and why the genus cannot be the cause of the species.

Core Idea
The genus cannot cause the species, otherwise it would be confined to a single species.

The philosophers extend the principle of interdependence to the logical components of a quiddity: genus and species. They argue that genus and species together form a single real quiddity, so there must be a causal relation between them. However, the genus cannot be the cause of the species, as that would limit the genus to that species. This lesson examines this argument and its implications for understanding composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage for this section
Focus on the part starting with 'قال الحكماء'. Notice the structure: claim, evidence, and conclusion.
The passage includes commentary within parentheses, but the main matn is clear.
2
Understand the unity of genus-species quiddity
The text states that the quiddity composed of genus and species is a single real unity. Grasp why this implies a need for causal relationship.
This is based on the view that parts of a real unity must have some dependency.
3
Identify the causal claim
One of the genus or species must be a cause of the other. The text explores which one can be the cause.
4
Understand why genus cannot cause species
If the genus were a cause of the species, it would entail that the genus is confined to a single species, which contradicts the nature of a genus being broader.
The text gives two expressions: 'it would entail it' and 'or we would say: the species...'
5
Reflect on the alternative wording
The author provides an alternative way to express the absurdity: 'or we would say the species...' This shows the forced choice.
The commentary clarifies that this is a choice between two ways of showing the corruption.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if genus and species are parts of a quiddity, the genus can be the cause of the species.
Correction: The text shows that if the genus caused the species, the genus would be confined to a single species, which is impossible because a genus naturally applies to multiple species.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for the genus to be a cause of the species?
A) Because the genus and species are the same thing.
B) Because if the genus caused the species, the genus would be confined to one species.
C) Because the cause must be external to the effect.
D) Because there is no need between genus and species.

Key Takeaway
In a real quiddity, genus and species must have a causal relation, but the genus cannot cause the species because it would restrict the genus to that species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your life, do you ever treat general categories (like 'culture,' 'religion,' or 'community') as if they directly cause specific behaviors in individuals? How might the lesson's principle—that the universal (genus) cannot cause the particular (species)—expose a flaw in your assumptions about people? Will you pause and reconsider one such assumption today?
?
You often define yourself by labels like 'Muslim,' 'student,' or 'daughter.' This lesson shows that a genus (like 'human') is not confined to one species. Are you allowing any label to confine your potential or determine your actions? What would it mean to see yourself as a species that specifies your genus, rather than being caused by it? Commit to one step to break free from a limiting assumption.
